## Thread: Issuing DOS commands, running Batch files, setting Environment Variables to persist, and starting applications, from COBOL. — 2 this month / 6 total

_Thread started [2011-07](2011-07.md#t-10fe2c9f41)._

#### ↳ Re: Issuing DOS commands, running Batch files, setting Environment Variables to persist, and starting applications, from COBOL.

- **From:** "HeyBub" <heybub@NOSPAMgmail.com>
- **Date:** 2011-08-01T07:21:18-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<EPmdnRxmkOqjB6vTnZ2dnUVZ_q-dnZ2d@earthlink.com>`
- **References:** `<99fa43F75lU1@mid.individual.net>`

```
Pete Dashwood wrote:
> A recent thread in this forum requested help with setting Environment
> Variables (EVs) from COBOL.
…[184 more quoted lines elided]…
> sometimes.

Hmm. Where's the documentation on invoking "WinShell" or ShellOBJ?

```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: Issuing DOS commands, running Batch files, setting Environment Variables to persist, and starting applications, from COBOL.

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2011-08-02T02:38:42+12:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<99ns3jFsnaU1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<99fa43F75lU1@mid.individual.net> <EPmdnRxmkOqjB6vTnZ2dnUVZ_q-dnZ2d@earthlink.com>`

```
HeyBub wrote:
> Pete Dashwood wrote:
>> A recent thread in this forum requested help with setting Environment
…[187 more quoted lines elided]…
> Hmm. Where's the documentation on invoking "WinShell" or ShellOBJ?

"WinShell" is a name I gave to the Windows COM component that has a ProgID 
of "Shell.Application". It is a user name for a Class. ALL COM components 
are Classes. In your programs you can give them any name you like,.just as 
you do with a file or a storage area.

"ShellObj" is also a user name as you can see by the fact that it is an 
object reference to an object  instance of Class WinShell. (I could have 
called it "Fred" or anything I liked, but COBOL encourages the use of 
meaningful names :-).)

(All of this is normal OO COBOL programming.)

I think maybe the question you are really asking is: "How did I know about 
this Class, as it isn't documented anywhere in the Fujitsu docs?"

I covered how you can find these things in another post.

There are HUNDREDS of useful COM components provided with Windows and 
THOUSANDS of useful .DLLs. But if people don't bother with OO programming 
they will never find them.

The real trick here is in using WSH to set a permanent EV instead of the 
temporary one set by COBOL or DOS BATCH (CMD.EXE). You CAN'T do it with the 
DOS SET command because that doesn't persist. (You can if you use Lee's 
convoluted generation of a DOS batch file from your app, but that is hardly 
an elegant solution).  When I learned C# and noticed how easy it was to 
access the Registry and set EVs, I suddenly realised that most (if not all) 
of the .Net facilities are available through Windows Script Hosting. All I 
had to do was break away from DOS the same way I broke away from COBOL.

(In 2011 it is high time all Windows Users broke away from DOS... :-)  It is 
really difficult... instead of using CMD you use CSCRIPT to execute. 
Suddenly you have everything you had in DOS, plus a whole lot more besides. 
BUT, you really need to understand Objects and Layers because the OS is 
built that way.) Compare the way the .wsf file creates an object instance of 
the Windows Shell with the way the COBOL program does it. Are they so 
dissimilar? Is there anything mysterious, inscrutable or difficult about it? 
It is tempting when you look at the 2 objects to think: Why not eliminate 
the script altogether and just set the User environment from OO COBOL in the 
COBOL program? You can certainly do that in C#. (You probably could in COBOL 
too, but I haven't investigated the Environment properties of the Shell 
Object created by COBOL. There are some advantages in having a separate 
script file because you can use it in other places, outside COBOL.)

By the way, nobody taught me these things, I just investigated them myself. 
Not something which is beyond most people.

The nice thng is that you don't have to understand the samples posted here 
in order to use them. They are simple and use less code than interfacing via 
the Win API. (I promise you will have less trouble interfacing to a COM 
component than you will in translating VB variables into COBOL. This is 
because the COM definitions are standardised and automatically "translated" 
by the COBOL compiler. For example if you define something as a string (PIC 
X(whatever)...) It gets passed to the COM Dispatch as type BSTRING,  PIC 
S9(5) COMP or BINARY or COMP-4 will be passed as type I2, etc. COBOL 
progammers don't have to know the COM data types because the COMPILER does 
the conversions under the covers. It doesn't do that for the Windows API.

There are around half a dozen lines in the COBOL program which are directly 
OO COBOL. You don't have to be deeply immersed in OO COBOL to be able to 
manipulate COM components with it, and anyone who has used PowerCOBOL to any 
extent has probably done exactly that (whether they realised it or not...)

Hope this helps. If it doesn't, try reading the other posts on it and if it 
is still unclear let me know and I'll do something on the COBOL 21 site by 
way of a step by step explanation.

Pete.
```

<a id="t-13c27f5afd"></a>
## Thread: Fujitsu Cobol - how to distribute .exe file? — 8 msgs

_Topics: [`Compilers and vendors`](topics.md#compilers) · [`Help requests and how-to`](topics.md#help)_

### Fujitsu Cobol - how to distribute .exe file?

- **From:** zalek <zalekbloom@hotmail.com>
- **Date:** 2011-08-03T16:25:36-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<81510285-a4ca-45fb-a2af-201c21fca0d2@f20g2000yqm.googlegroups.com>`

```
I am using Fujitsu Cobol - when I run a program on my PC - it runs OK.
I found the PATH points to a folder with Fujitsu Cobol .dll files. How
can I build a Fujitsu Cobol .exe object that will include all
required .DLL files, so it can run it on PC where Fujitsu Cobol is not
installed?
I search manual, but did not found solution.

Thanks,

Zalek
```

#### ↳ Re: Fujitsu Cobol - how to distribute .exe file?

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2011-08-04T12:35:57+12:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<99u7rcF4pmU1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<81510285-a4ca-45fb-a2af-201c21fca0d2@f20g2000yqm.googlegroups.com>`

```
zalek wrote:
> I am using Fujitsu Cobol - when I run a program on my PC - it runs OK.
> I found the PATH points to a folder with Fujitsu Cobol .dll files. How
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
> Zalek

Generally, you would use the Fujitsu installer to install the Fujitsu run 
time libraries as part of your distribution.

This is a Fujitsu supplied .MSI package. There are several and it depends on 
what facilities you are using which one you would deploy.

The methods for deployment change across versions of Fujitsu COBOL so you 
need to check the documentation for the version you are using.

When you installed the development environment it should also have installed 
the deployment support but it depends on the version.

The Runtime .MSI is designed so that if there is already a Fujitsu runtime 
on the target machine, it wont install again (unless it is an older version 
and then it prompts.)

To do what you asked (building all the called .DLLs into the executable) 
would need a specific linkage editor run and it will be a lot of grief. Some 
of the Libraries are dependent and are called dynamically if/when needed. It 
COULD be done, but life is too short and Fujitsu provide all of these 
libraries packaged for install as a Runtime .MSI.

There are alternatives to the Fujitsu installer (Inno Install is very good 
one which I have found useful in the past) but anything you use will need 
the Fujitsu runtime.

Please state which version of Fujitsu you are using if you need further 
help.

Pete.
```

- **Message-ID:** `<cn1l3797fg8n91b1qmpi0k6goc5eu8rhl5@4ax.com>`
- **References:** `<81510285-a4ca-45fb-a2af-201c21fca0d2@f20g2000yqm.googlegroups.com> <99u7rcF4pmU1@mid.individual.net>`

```
My problem is little unconventional.
We have on our network few PCs. I don't want to install my program on

install myprogram.exe program on X: drive, without changing PCs
configuration and if a PC wants to run my program - on a DOS screen it
will issue command:
X:\myprogram.exe

This is the reason I want to put all .dll in my .exe object.

Zalek
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Fujitsu Cobol - how to distribute .exe file?

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2011-08-05T03:02:13+12:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<99vqjpFccuU1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<81510285-a4ca-45fb-a2af-201c21fca0d2@f20g2000yqm.googlegroups.com> <99u7rcF4pmU1@mid.individual.net> <cn1l3797fg8n91b1qmpi0k6goc5eu8rhl5@4ax.com>`

```
ZalekBloom@hotmail.com wrote:
> On Thu, 4 Aug 2011 12:35:57 +1200, "Pete Dashwood"
> <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
…[56 more quoted lines elided]…
> Zalek

I understand. This is a normal shared application running over the LAN. You 
need to have your .EXE program installed on the server (X: drive, shared). 
However, you still need the Fujitsu runtime installed (definitely on the 
server, and also on any machines you want it to run on.). This is because 
the executable gets copied to the machine which is sharing the X: drive. 
(There is no true remote execution in a DOS LAN... it just looks that way. 
You CAN use Remote Procedure Calls via DCOM to execute remote COM 
components, but that is a horse of a different colour...))
As soon as your executable makes a call to one of the runtime .DLLs it will 
fail, unless you provide the runtime .DLLs on the local machine. You should 
see a message saying tha .DLL cannot be found. It will be F3B<whatever> or 
possibly one of the F5 group of libraries, depending on your Fujitsu 
version.

Note that you only need to install the runtime ONCE on each machine. After 
that you can deploy COBOL apps without needing to redeploy the runtime.

I think what you are looking for is something like the old Micro Focus COBOL 
(Workbench - 16 bit) which could be built into an executable and deployed 
without further support. Those days are long gone. Both Micro Focus and 
Fujitsu now use a "runtime" architecture and it must be deployed with any 
executables (.DLL or .EXE) you install. (Micro Focus charge a licence fee 
for the use of their runtime every time you deploy it; Fujitsu do not.).

Given that you pay a lot of money for a full COBOL compiler and support, you 
might reasonably expect to be able to deploy the work you do with it, 
without having to pay a royalty. This point has been raised many times with 
Micro Focus, both here and elsewhere and they are adamant that the royalties 
are an essential part of their revenue stream. Of course, the latest .Net 
COBOL compilers generate CIL  (just as C# and VB.Net do) and this is then 
translated by the Common Language Runtime (CLR) into native code for the 
platform, so they don't need a runtime. (The "runtime" is provided by the 
.Net Framework, which is provided free by those nice people at Microsoft... 
:-)) Some people avoid the whole problem by using VB.Net or C#, which are 
also free.

I'm sure you have the deployableable runtime package, although you may not 
recognise it. As requested earlier, if you state what version of Fujitsu you 
are using, I can tell you what to look for.

Pete.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Fujitsu Cobol - how to distribute .exe file?

_(thread depth: 5)_

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2011-08-07T16:38:03+12:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<9a6j5hF9gpU1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<81510285-a4ca-45fb-a2af-201c21fca0d2@f20g2000yqm.googlegroups.com> <99u7rcF4pmU1@mid.individual.net> <cn1l3797fg8n91b1qmpi0k6goc5eu8rhl5@4ax.com> <99vqjpFccuU1@mid.individual.net> <mfqr37tsf4s0589hh2q6dlp0a7rcstla4s@4ax.com>`

```
ZalekBloom@hotmail.com wrote:
> On Fri, 5 Aug 2011 03:02:13 +1200, "Pete Dashwood"
> <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
…[114 more quoted lines elided]…
> anything on PC which is not under my authority is a nightmare.

I understand what you're saying, but the runtime is really just part of your 
application. If they want the application, they have to let you install 
it... :-)

As you are trying to run it remotely, it looks like they may not want the 
application installed either.

>
> Which versions of Fujitsu Cobol create CIL? My Fujitsu Cobol is called
> Netcobol - does it means it is .Net?

No. "NetCOBOL for .Net" is the stupid and confusing name that Fujitsu chose 
for their their latest CIL generating OO COBOL compiler.

NetCOBOL is the standard, unmanaged code, Fujitsu OO COBOL compiler. It is a 
very good implementation of the language. I believe the current version is 
v10 although I stopped using it after v7. v3 was a free one which did not 
have everything in it. v4, v6, v7, and v9 were all stable and offered more 
facilities and bug fixes. This is why I aske you for a version; deployment 
of apps and runtimes was changed across some of these versions.

From v6 they moved to the CASPER remote server registration which was 
supposed to prevent piracy but is easily circumvented by people who know 
what they're doing (and they are the people MOST likely to sell pirate 
copies). For normal legitimate users and small businesses this just 
increased the risk of using COBOL; transferring your licence to a different 
machine or reloading an image copy became a nightmare and it just wasn't 
worth the trouble. If your business depended on COBOL (as mine did at the 
time) it was a severe worry to find that a server problem with CASPER could 
result in you being unable to use software you had bought and paid for.

(Aside: I recently worked with some clients to develop a software protection 
system that prevents pirate copies of their applications being run. (Remote 
Application Validation - RAV).  Like CASPER, it does use a web service to 
validate that the software is legitimate. BUT, if you buy the software 
outright, it gets validated once and after that you can do whatever you like 
with it. If you copy it, it will still run but not on someone else's site. 
If you need to move it to a different machine you can (and it remains 
running on the old machine) but , it has to be fingerprinted to the new 
machine. (Your vendor does this remotely, and may or may not charge you for 
it.) I used all the things I hated about CASPER as design criteria for what 
to avoid... :-)) RAV (the PRIMA software) is simple for users and is 
currently in its first live implementations. For software houses developing 
packages they plan to lease, this is an ideal tool. It is written entirely 
in C# but can protect software written in any language that supports COM, 
including Fujitsu and Micro Focus COBOL or .Net COBOL)

NetCOBOL for .Net is apparently very good but it costs around $8000 for a 
compiler which does what C# does for free. Alchemy are not disposed to 
refund your money or even give you a credit for other software if you change 
your mind. I know of at least one very angry customer who was told he needed 
the product then found he didn't... no refunds or credits.

> Meantime I am thinking of rewritting my program in C# or VBScript.

Obviously, I would say C# :-)  However, VB.Net may be good if you already 
have VB experience. You can certainly use VBScript (or JavaScript) across 
the LAN; just wrap it in WSH.

It all comes down to exactly what you want to do and how much LAN is 
involved.

Good luck!

Pete.
```

#### ↳ Re: COBOL compiler for Solaris SPARC?

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2011-08-15T01:51:57+12:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<9aq27vFda6U1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<cb8a0ff62486b32c9b312ae80148f6ae@msgid.frell.theremailer.net>`

```
Fritz Wuehler wrote:
> Can anyone recommend a COBOL compiler for Solaris SPARC? I see
> Alchemy has one but I read a pretty serious asskicking by Pete on
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
> gents. an only speak from what I know.

I can only speak from what I know. I don't know which asskicking you're 
referring to, but I don't come here specifically to kick asses. Having said 
that, if I see something I think is bad, I'll say so. Generally, I call them 
like I see them and I have no axe to grind.

Here's a very short expression of my opinion on this (Please note this is 
OPINION, it is arguable...):

1. Both Fujitsu and Micro Focus have COBOL versions that run on Solaris.

2. Both companies have excellent products, however, having used both, I 
would go for Fujitsu.

3. In terms of support I have been extremely impressed by Micro Focus who 
are interested and supportive through their forums. Every problem I had 
which I couldn't fix myself, they fixed in a friendly and timely way. And 
there was no charge, even though I was evaluating their software and had not 
bought it. It was exemplary. (I contributed to their forum with a script for 
batch compiling COM components as a "thank you". A couple of hundred people 
looked at or downloaded it.)

4. Cannot say the same for Alchemy. They did a cost cutting exercise a while 
back (about the time they did the deal with Fujitsu) and fired people who 
really knew their stuff (one of them posted here recently). As a result, 
their "support" suffered to the point where I decided to undertake my own 
support of the product and spent considerable time becoming familiar enough 
with it to be able to do that. I haven't dealt with them for about four 
years and the last time I did, they pissed me off so badly I moved off COBOL 
as a development tool.(They did me a favour; every cloud has a silver 
lining.). The attitude I encountered was very much one of "you are locked in 
to COBOL and we are the only game in town". I had allocated and budgeted 
funds for Net COBOL for .Net but when I went to buy it I was treated very 
shabbily and it was like I was expected to beg them to supply it to me. (A 
strange concept of customer service...)  Turned out I didn't need it anyway 
as all my legacy COBOL runs fine on .Net using InterOP services, after being 
refactored or wrapped as objects. It can be maintained with the standard 
NetCOBOL and PowerCOBOL compilers which I already have. I'm gradually 
replacing them with C# but the standard (free) Microsoft options  (C#, 
Visual Studio, .Net, InterOP services) buy me all the time I need to do 
this. The alternative was to move to Micro Focus, and although they are a 
good company, there's no  way I will pay in principle for a run time every 
time I distribute an application.

5. Since 2007, Open COBOL has supported Solaris.

6. ORACLE ProCOBOL also runs on Solaris.

Bottom line:  The best product is Fujitsu but the American vendor (Alchemy) 
leaves much to be desired. An alternative would be Micro Focus but you must 
license the runtime and pay a royalty every time you distribute an 
application. Open COBOL is probably not yet "Industrial Strength" (They 
don't support Objects. That's a show-stopper for me...)  ORACLE is probably 
not cheap, but neither are the others, except Open COBOL.

Invest the time and learn C#  (It's fun and it is free :-)) You can learn it 
from free videos (I did...) and possibly the odd book as a reference. I 
recommend the WROX series "C# Professional", but the videos are really good 
and mostly free. (If you download Visual C# from Microsoft the startup page 
of Visual Studio points you at some free "getting started" videos.) Anything 
you get stuck with there is instant help available, along with sample code, 
from the multi-million user base via the Internet.

COBOL, according to the Tiobe base, is now 47th programming language and 
falling. (Last time I looked, about 18 months ago, it was 30th).

C# is 5th and rising.

This only matters if you are looking for a job.

Good luck with your search. Let us know how you get on and what you do in 
the end.

Pete.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: COBOL compiler for Solaris SPARC?

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2011-08-16T03:31:23+12:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<9assedFkk1U1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<9aq27vFda6U1@mid.individual.net> <d45bb6b1cc8b29cacba493b71e5ffd21@dizum.com>`

```
Nomen Nescio wrote:
>> I can only speak from what I know. I don't know which asskicking
>> you're referring to, but I don't come here specifically to kick
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
> correct me if I was wrong!

I think it is covered by "I call 'em as I see 'em".

I've been doing that for nearly 20 years now  right here in this forum (and 
its predecessor), and it has upset people from time to time. In my own 
defence all I will say is that I don't post with malice. I try to be fair 
and I am always honest, as far as I see it.

I PREFER to be positive about products and companies but sometimes they get 
it so badly wrong it is pretty hard  to say anything positive about them. 
(Fortunately, that is rare.)
>
>> 2. Both companies have excellent products, however, having used
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> are talking about when it comes to this sort of thing.

Thanks for the vote of confidence. :-)  I have been dealing with COBOL and 
IT for 46 years across a number of continents and in many diverse companies 
so you'd hope I mght have learned something... :-) However, as I said 
before, what I'm writing here is opinion (I like to think it is informed 
opinion),so it is arguable.

>> 3. In terms of support I have been extremely impressed by Micro
>> Focus who are interested and supportive through their forums. Every
…[11 more quoted lines elided]…
> been sold.

I wasn't aware they have been sold (again...). Are you referring to Liant? 
As of yesterday (15th August, 2011) they are in discussions regarding a 
possible purchase by "private equity", but no deal has been struck as far as 
I am aware.

Certainly, being taken over and sold several times has affected the company 
but some people would argue it has become stronger. I agree with you about 
the focus, inasmuch as all the eggs are now in the basket of Visual COBOL (a 
.Net CIL generating compiler). This is a bit of a knife edge, for reasons I 
shall now discuss.

Their customer base is mainframe sites like Banks who have a heavy 
investment in COBOL and are looking to move it to the Network. These are 
dyed-in-the-wool procedural COBOL shops who are being dragged kicking and 
screaming into objects. Visual COBOL supports objects (it would be 
unthinkable for a .Net product NOT to...) so Micro Focus are offering tools 
to convert JCL and CICS which are familiar to these people, as a sweet way 
to get them in, without too much stress on the need for objects and layers. 
(In fairness, Alchemy also offer tools to do these conversions.) That's all 
very well, but the reason I say it is a knife edge is because it gradually 
becomes apparent that trying to run the Network as a Batch COBOL 
environment is disastrous. The concepts and methods of working which are 
fine for a centralised processor running a procedural language, are NOT fine 
for a Network of distributed processors. It needs an Object Oriented 
language. Fine, Visual COBOL is Object Oriented , so that's all right then. 
Only, it isn't.

Inevitably, the nettle has to be grasped and they have to start looking at 
SaaS and  Web Services and getting to grips with objects and layers. Slowly, 
certain legacy code gets refactored or re-structured to run as OO COBOL.

If their programmers have been exposed to Java it won't be SO traumatic. (If 
they haven't, they can take solace in the fact that they can still write 
COBOL and it compiles for .Net. Only, performance is crap and if they want 
it to be better they have to start understanding the Framework. That means 
getting to grips with Classes and Objects...)

After about a year the programmers are starting to realise that programming 
objects in COBOL is like wading through treacle. All the hype around the new 
cool Visual COBOL is starting to sound a bit thin. (Most of the "benefits" 
being touted for it come from the use of Visual Studio 2010 and are 
available with the free .Net languages, as well as with the expensive .Net 
COBOL)

Management are starting to see COBOL maintenance in the new environment as 
"expensive" (just like it was in the old one) and upgrades and licenses cost 
an arm and a leg. Then someone has the light bulb over their head light up.

They can get all the benfits of .Net WITHOUT all the costs of COBOL if they 
simply move off COBOL.

All it takes is a one time investment in some training. Programmers LOVE 
being trained in cool stuff at company expense and it improves morale no 
end. The old-timers all scream "Heresy!" and start turning widdershins to 
appease the Lords of COBOL, but the youngsters embrace it readily. (They 
learned about objects and layers at school and had to "unlearn" it to deal 
with COBOL.)

Micro Focus can no longer offer them anything they want. Both Management and 
Programmers are finding it costs less and takes less effort to develop for 
.Net in a language designed for doing that. Programmers find their value 
increases, (and, consequently, their job options...), Management find their 
maintenance costs go down (significantly...), everybody is happy (except 
Micro Focus.)

Visual COBOL fell off the knife edge just as ANY COBOL will . It isn't a 
reflection on the quality of Micro Focus or ANY company that is marketing 
COBOL as it's main attraction. It is a recognition that the COBOL boat has 
sailed. However, during the next few years there is still a market for COBOL 
vendors. It depends how long the market takes to wake up.

I won't personally be buying shares in Micro Focus, although I wish them 
well.




>
>> 4. Cannot say the same for Alchemy. They did a cost cutting exercise
…[26 more quoted lines elided]…
> their support was outstanding so perhaps it's worth it.

The support comes through their forum. It is like a user group. However, 
when I had some trouble getting COM to work it was MF employees who came to 
the rescue. They were competent, informed, and friendly.

> I really
> wouldn't know until much later after I could come up with a pricing
…[10 more quoted lines elided]…
>

The last I know they were running an automated system where you post your 
problem  (as a registered user) and they get back to you.

No Comment. (Hopefully, they have packed it in now.)


>

Mono (a .Net Framework for Nix systems) runs on Solaris and C# runs in Mono. 
(I have tested this on Linux and was amazed... :-)) In fact, Solaris 
recently implemented a version of Silverlight (they call it Moonlight) which 
uses all the really cool XAML (platform independence) for the presentation 
layer. I like Windows Forms but Silverlight is better in many ways. C# 
integrates with it seamlessly. I have grasped that you want to use COBOL 
:-), but let's keep the facts straight... :-)



I agree Java and C# are good general
> purpose choices but I feel Java isn't a good tool for vendors to
> write software since it can be reversed rather easily and all your
> hard work goes down the drain. Not sure if this is true of C# as well.

If by "reversed" you mean "reverse engineered" that applies to ANY language 
you write in, including COBOL. Object code can be disassembled  and changed. 
Java may be considered more vulnerabe because it generates intermediate byte 
code which is JIT compiled by the JVM, just like C# and VB.Net generate 
intermediate CIL which is JIT compiled by the .Net Framework CLR, but. 
ultimately, if you are worried about people reverse engineering your code, 
you would need to take measures in that code which are irrespective of the 
language you write it in.
```

```
all, and that is why I couldn't find a place to download or even buy it. It
seems to be a preprocessor for COBOL programs that Oracle supplies with
Oracle DB so you can embed SQL in COBOL code, nothing more. That is
certainly an important value added item for Oracle DB but it is no COBOL
compiler! We're back to the short list of products again. Can you confirm
this, Pete, or am I all wet?

> If by "reversed" you mean "reverse engineered" that applies to ANY language 
> you write in, including COBOL.

I really don't agree with that from a practical standpoint. Note, I did get
paid in several jobs to reverse engineer OS code so I'm more familiar with
this topic than average, at least on IBM systems. I do realize there are
many tools for PC hackers wanting to disassemble C/C++ etc but those tools
don't exist for the mainframe and they're not very practical because people
who have the resources to develop mainframe software or buy and use it
aren't in the business of ripping each other off, thankfully there's too
much at stake.

> Object code can be disassembled  and changed.

Yes, certainly, but you're taking a big big chance with your career doing
that in the environment I've worked in. I don't know any case of that ever
happening, except within the company that wrote the code to begin with or in
our case not reversing it for the sake of ripping it off or modifying it but
rather to install extensions to processing, user exits etc. that didn't
exist. What I'm talking about with Java is I understand it's all too easy to
completely recover somebody's code, much easier than in any compiled
language, and I don't want that.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: COBOL compiler for Solaris SPARC?

```
Nomen Nescio wrote:
> "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
>
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> FWIW I agree with your self-appraisal.

Thanks :-)
>
>> I wasn't aware they have been sold (again...). Are you referring to
…[19 more quoted lines elided]…
>> Yes. :-)  But not a lot.

I have used it with Micro Focus COBOL, and, yes, it is a pre-compiler. I 
noted it in passing as we were talking about Solaris.
>
> I did a bit more looking and it seems to me that Pro*COBOL is not
…[17 more quoted lines elided]…
> ripping each other off, thankfully there's too much at stake.

Hmmm... it is dangerous to assume, when it comes to "people". :-)

>
>> Object code can be disassembled  and changed.
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> processing, user exits etc. that didn't exist.

I keep getting an image of Vizzini saying "Inconceivable!"...)

Mainframe code can certainly be disassembled just like any other. It can be 
patched and changed just like any other (whether you are doing it to "extend 
and enhance" or whether you are doing it to bypass security features...) I 
think we have to agree to disagree on this.


What I'm talking about
> with Java is I understand it's all too easy to completely recover
> somebody's code, much easier than in any compiled language, and I
> don't want that.

What is important to you, is important to you. I've never let the thought 
that someone could reverse engineer my software (on ANY platform) deter me 
from producing it. If I was nervous about this there are facilities (like 
reflection in C#) I can take advantage of  to make it harder, but I've never 
done that. In a way, if someone decides to appropriate your effort and use 
it for their own purposes, that is kind of flattering. :-) But you see them 
in Court... :-) (Or you "send some of the boys around..." :-))

There is often a smugness about the posts we see here from mainframers. 
"Mainframes don't have viruses"... "Mainframes are more secure" ... 
"Mainframe code cannot be hacked" ... etc. (Now... "Mainframers don't 
reverse engineer software"... :-))  Even if these claims were all true (and 
the "truth" of them is relative; all of the above are certainly possible 
and, in some cases, on record as having happened...) it is foolish to adopt 
these positions and do nothing to address them, if there is a real risk.

The bottom line is that if something is of concern to you, you should 
address it, irrespective of what platform you are working on.

I started computing when there were only mainframes. I worked on them for 
over 20 years (not just IBM ones). I have done things using privileged 
instructions and OS exits (like ESTAE) which completely changed the 
behaviour of the software as it was written and allowed it to do things the 
author never intended. :-) (Some of this was prompted by curiosity, some of 
it by mischief, and some of it by necessity... :-)) The bottom line is that 
NO Von Neumann platform is totally immune from attack. Not even a mainframe. 
However, isolating your processor from remote access (as mainframes often 
are) is a step in the right direction as far as security goes.

Pete.
```

```
If you can live within COBOL 85 then OpenCobol is a good compiler with
a superb manual. In fact I suggest downloading
the  OC manual even if you use something else. It has a smattering of
COBOL 2000 features but I generally ignore them.

As for object features etc. if you want OO programming I suggest you
use a language built around that concept. COBOL with OO
features is making a sows ear out of a silk purse, IMO of course.

John Culleton
COBOLing since 1968.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: COBOL compiler for Solaris SPARC?

_(thread depth: 4)_

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2011-08-20T15:20:07+12:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<9b8nf9FpatU1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<1313349523@f1.n250.z2.fidonet.ftn> <2685a1cdd4fdc980ffff3d50c4179f57@msgid.frell.theremailer.net> <ccd01e5c-aaf9-4552-bcf8-77486532fbf4@y4g2000vbx.googlegroups.com>`

```
john@wexfordpress.com wrote:
> On Aug 15, 2:19 pm, Fritz Wuehler
> <fr...@spamexpire-201108.rodent.frell.theremailer.net> wrote:
…[18 more quoted lines elided]…
> features is making a sows ear out of a silk purse, IMO of course.

I agree completely.

The features were added (and it was a superb job of software engineering) so 
that people would not HAVE to move off COBOL when they went to OO 
environments.

Sadly, the uptake was poor (most COBOLlers had no idea why you might want or 
need Classes and Objects) and by the time people started to think about 
using Objects, COBOL had been overtaken by languages designed for (and 
therefore better suited to) an Object environment. I used Fujitsu Object 
COBOL for nearly 10 years (mainly to write components that could run on the 
web or a desktop) and found it excellent.

Eventually I moved to Java and C# and the scales fell off my eyes. It is 
just SO much more productive and less verbose than using COBOL.

Currently, there are expensive compilers available for COBOL to compile CIL 
for .Net. I believe they are pretty much pointless. If you want that 
environment use a language intended for it. (C# and VB.Net are free...) 
Legacy COBOL can be easily wrapped using InterOP services and there is no 
need to perpetuate non-object code into an Object environment.

In a way, although I greatly admire the people who did it, it can be argued 
in retrospect that adding OO facilities to COBOL was a bad move.

Without it, people would have needed to explore other options which, as it 
turns out, are actually better.

Pete.
```

#### ↳ Re: COBOL compiler for Solaris SPARC?

- **From:** Alistair Maclean <alistair.j.l.maclean@gmail.com>
- **Date:** 2011-08-15T07:05:58-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<5f8c75b9-01da-4373-8ac5-8c79b93dceb1@t7g2000vbv.googlegroups.com>`
- **References:** `<cb8a0ff62486b32c9b312ae80148f6ae@msgid.frell.theremailer.net>`

```
In looking at another reply in this thread (the one that mentions
Veryant's isCOBOL Evolve) it crossed my mind that you may be
constrained in your choice of compiler by the file structures that it
will be required to work with. ISAM files vary in their content from
one supplier to the next; Fujitsu and Micro Focus are not compatible
(in my experience) ie you can't take a Fujitsu ISAM file and run a
Micro Focus program against it and vice versa. Also, if there is one
intended RDBMS to be used then it may be a good idea to check that the
ODBC drivers are available under Linux/Unix for the RDBMS/COBOL
combination that you opt for.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Visual Cobol R4

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2011-08-17T12:26:08+12:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<9b0g53FuthU1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<076d5cef-478d-4bfc-b69c-ffc1ec41c3bb@glegroupsg2000goo.googlegroups.com> <9ave13Frp8U1@mid.individual.net> <9ec207f6-8a7b-47a1-9df6-ddd7e8f2909e@gz5g2000vbb.googlegroups.com>`

```
Cl�udio Miguel M�ller wrote:
> Hi Pete,
> sorry my english.

No problem, (it is much better than my Portuguese... :-)) We just need to be 
sure that you are understood.
>
> I already use Visual Cobol R4 to run simples Cobol programs caracter
> display.

OK, these are called "Desktop" or "Console" applications. As opposed to GUI 
(Graphic User Interface) applications.
>
> But i dont know to use Visual Cobol with Visual Studio IDE.

I understand. Visual Studio is pretty huge and it is very powerful. BUT, it 
does make designing and building GUI applications very easy in whatever 
language you are using (including COBOL). You already know COBOL but you 
probably need to invest some time in learning to use Visual Studio. (It's a 
bit like learning to use a chainsaw if you are a lumberjack...)

You can use Visual Studio (VS) to design and build Windows forms by dragging 
and dropping form controls (text boxes, dropdowns, menus, tree views, 
buttons, images, etc. onto the form surface from a toolbox, in VS. VS gives 
you access to all the properties (colour, font, etc.) of each control and 
you can also access these properties from your COBOL. This is all done in a 
part of VS called the "Design Surface" or "Designer". You can see exactly 
what you are building and you can test run it even before you write any 
COBOL. Once you are satisfied with your form the next step is to write the 
event processing in COBOL. Events are "what happens". The user clicks a 
button on your form and a "Click" event is raised. The system vectors 
control to the code you wrote to handle that event. (If you didn't write 
any, the results are disappointing... nothing happens when the button is 
clicked, for example...)

This is a different paradigm from the usual COBOL one where the programmer 
controls the order in which things happen (open file, read data, process 
data, close file...) The GUI environment is event driven and your code must 
respond when events happen. Although this is a little strange at first, it 
is real fun to write and very satisfying when it is finished. VS lets you 
step through the code for each event as it occurs and it isn't too long 
before you are productive.

>
> I dont know to use Windows Forms in Visual Cobol R4 IDE.

No, you need to learn how to use Visual Studio. There are basic videos 
available that will get you started, but the ones I used are in English. You 
probably need to search for tutorial videos in Portuguese.
>
> I dont find examples and/or tutoriais to create Windows Forms.

It is really pretty easy once you master the tools. I strongly recommend 
video as the way to learn. You can pause it and back up if you hit something 
you are not sure about, and there are thousands of free short videos 
available on the Internet. You may not find examples specifically for COBOL, 
but the principles are the same for whatever language you are using.  (The 
Design Surface is language independent and it doesn't matter whether you use 
VB, C#, C++, or COBOL you still build your forms the same way.)

> Examples in Visual Cobol R4 is not completed or with more details.
>

It would be very hard for them to show the non-COBOL part of GUI development 
unless they gave a video or a detailed walkthrough. Get to know VS and what 
you want to do will fall into place.

Good Luck!

Pete.
```

### Ot: The Last Indignity

- **From:** "Charles Hottel" <chottel@earthlink.net>
- **Date:** 2011-08-18T00:38:48-04:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<BYednfgxhfC6CtHTnZ2dnUVZ_uKdnZ2d@earthlink.com>`

```
Remember the scene at the end of the  Donnie Brasco film where he receives a 
$500 check and a medal from the FBI in return for risking his life every day 
in an undercover assignment?  Also the executive could not correctly 
pronounce his last name. Well at my agency there was no check but they did 
give me a nice watch and a plaque detailing my years of service to the U.S. 
government and to my agency.  Of course to receive this I had to fill out a 
form with all of the information and the form had to be approved by my 
supervisor.  This was how I found out I had a new supervisor.  Anyway I put 
my job title on the form as "Information Technology Specialist" but my 
plaque has "Information Technician Specialist" printed on it.  Every time I 
see this I am irked by it.

Yeah I know get over it alread, it really does not matter.  Ok, ok I know 
nobody cares and you all wish I would shut up about it.  It is enough 
already!  This is my last complaint along these lines.  Just be greatful 
that over the years I have quiet about numerous management screwups and 
incompentent decisions.  I guess my experience with management leads me more 
toward the Doc Dwarf view.

I hope all of you are more fortunate whenever you retire and that you will 
all feel appreciated.
```

```
Charles Hottel wrote:
> Remember the scene at the end of the  Donnie Brasco film where he
> receives a $500 check and a medal from the FBI in return for risking
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
> time I see this I am irked by it.

Charlie,

You have a right to be irked by it.  Send it back and ask them politely if 
it might be possible to get it changed. Tell them you appreciate the thought 
and value it as a symbol of their esteem but it is irritating when it is 
incorrect.

In the unlikely event that they can't or won't repair it, chuck it in the 
bin and console yourself with the thought that you don't really need their 
approval to have done your best. You did the job; they paid you. Let it go.


>
> Yeah I know get over it alread, it really does not matter.

It DOES matter insofar as, if they are going to show a token of their 
appreciation, they should get it right. Better nothing at all than something 
that is flawed.

Sure, they don't HAVE to do anything, but as they HAVE done something they 
should invest a little effort and get it right.

Don't take it to heart, or take it personally. You have worked there long 
enough to know they are not always right on the ball. It wasn't done with 
malice, it is just incompetence. Be glad you're not part of it any more.





> Ok, ok I
> know nobody cares and you all wish I would shut up about it.

Not me. I'm very happy to hear you express what you think. But remember that 
how you feel about it is over to you. (If it is any consolation, I'd be 
pissed off if it was me and I'd ask them to fix it. Politely, and nicely, 
but I'd still ask...)


> It is
> enough already!  This is my last complaint along these lines.  Just
> be greatful that over the years I have quiet about numerous
> management screwups and incompentent decisions.

Did they extend you the same courtesy :-)?

There is an argument that says if you DON'T speak up when you see bad 
management it will never get fixed.

There is another argument that says if you DO speak up when you see bad 
management you may be out of a job. :-)

As DD has sagely observed here on more than one occasion: you can be fired 
for being wrong, but you can also be fired for being right...

The best approach is to try and cultivate a skill set that ensures you can 
always get a job, then speak your mind. Tell the truth and shame the Devil. 
Rock the boat, get things changed. Make it a better place to work and be 
glad to work there. If that is not a viable course of action for you, then 
you have two options:

1. Leave.
2. Stop worrying about it. Keep a low profile, don't let it get to you, and 
take the money.

If you prefer to have a quiet life and wait it out for retirement, then 
there is really no point in having any opinion about anything that is likely 
to rock the boat. Keep your head down, do the miles, then take the pension 
and don't really give a damn whether they give you a watch or a plaque or 
lunch with the team, or nothing at all. Remember, you don't need their 
approval; just their paychecks... :-)

Try not to feel bad, Charlie. Look on it as a new phase of your life where 
you can do stuff without having to answer to some incompetent COI.

I wish you every good thing for the future.


> I guess my
> experience with management leads me more toward the Doc Dwarf view.
>

Oh No!!! Anything but that!!! :-)

Doc isn't always right, but he isn't always wrong, either. :-)

Unless you made some effort to change things, you have no moral right to 
criticise. And since when did you need the approval of people who you have a 
low opinion of?

Despite what you may think, not ALL management is bad but each of us will 
form an opinion based on our own experience. I have worked with good and bad 
managers and I've tried to learn from both types... :-)


> I hope all of you are more fortunate whenever you retire and that you
> will all feel appreciated.

Personally, I don't need to feel appreciated (although its always nice when 
people say "Thank you"), but thanks anyway :-)

Pete.
```

```
wife is 24 years younger than me and as long as she does not remarry before 
age 55 she will get a benefit of 55% of my full retirement.  Of course my 
retirement is reduced by the amount necessary to pay for her benefit.  I am 
sure that what they take out does not cover the entire thing if she lives a

easier live 30 years or more after my death.  They are few defined benefit 
retirment plans these days and even fewer as good as what the government is

leader was my supervisor.  Then the government decided to reduce the layers 
of management and project leaders were no longer official supervisors. 
However in practice they did many supervisory functions.  For a while they 
even wrote up the performance reviews and my real supervisor signed them. 
Later on the real supervisor sometimes wrote the reviews.  Most of the time, 
99.99%, I only communicated directly with my project leader.  My project 
leader had less than 10 people under him but my actual supervisor might have 
50 or more below him.  This got worse when whe became part of Homeland 
Security because management had more higher up manager to deal with. The 
simple fact is that my supervisor had to much to deal with in any detail and 
no time to deal directly with peons like me.  I kept my project leader 
informed in detail but God only knows what he communicated to my supervisor. 
My project leader had his failings but he was one of the few non-asshole 
managers.  From my vantage point ass-holedness seemded to be a prime 
requirement for managerial advancement.  The bigger asshole you were the 
greater your chances of advancement.

the old system so there was little new work being done to the old system.  I 
did learn Java thinking I could work on the replacement system, but all new 
programming for the new system was done by a consortium of contractors.
```

#### ↳ Re: Ot: The Last Indignity

- **From:** billg999@cs.uofs.edu (Bill Gunshannon)
- **Date:** 2011-08-18T15:16:15+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<9b4olvFe0eU1@mid.individual.net>`

```
In article <BYednfgxhfC6CtHTnZ2dnUVZ_uKdnZ2d@earthlink.com>,
	"Charles Hottel" <chottel@earthlink.net> writes:
> Remember the scene at the end of the  Donnie Brasco film where he receives a 
> $500 check and a medal from the FBI in return for risking his life every day 
…[18 more quoted lines elided]…
> all feel appreciated. 
 
Hmmm......

I just came to the end of my Army career.  It has spanned 43 years.
I have been promoted (as an enlisted man, an NCO and an Officer) 
about 10 times.  I have never been publicly recognized for any of
these achievments.  

Among my awards I have 2 Army Commendation Medals.  The first was presented
in a closed office with 4 people present and one of them was "the official
photographer" who took pictures no one ever saw.  My second was even better.
I was awarded this one for my mobilization which included trips to Korea,
Qatar, Kuwait, Iraq and Djibouti.  Several months after my activation was
over I came home with my wife and after spotting a padded envelope laying
on the porch she asked if i had ordered anything.  I said, "No", so she
picked it up and opened it.  It was my Army Commendation Medal.

Retirement?  My official retirement date was 28 July 2011.  As far as I
have been able to find out, they haven't even filed the paperwork yet.
It's a safe bet that there will be no official recognition of this
achievement either.  I just wish they would get it done so I could get
my retirement pay and medical benefits that I worked so long for.

Oh, and one more from long ago just to show that nothing has really
changed.  From 1980 to 1984 I worked as an NCO at the United States
Military Academy.  In March of 1984 I got out of the Army and became
a government contractor with Martin Marietta, still at USMA.  Over
my years there I had attended many departure luncheons.  The normal
practice was to give the departee a nice plaque that was made from a
polished disk platter encircling an emblem from a Cadet "Tar Bucket"
headgear.  They are really very nice.  My departure came and went.
Nothing.  Around June of that year a SP/4 from computer operations
comes waltzing into my office.  He has one of these plaques in has
hands.  He walks up to my desk and say, "Hey, we found this in the
office.  It has your name on it.  Do you want it?"  How's that for
a presentation?

Take your retirement check and be happy.

bill
```

```
I feel you disappoiuntment.  Sorry you received such poor treatment.  I did 
get a 30 yers tie clasp pin. It appeared on day on my desk.  Also from 1992 
up to 2001 my job was very different.

I did receive some cash awards for several years.  While I do think I 
deserved them I also believe that part of the reason I got some of them was 
because all of the other programmers on my team were contractors who were 
not eligible to receive a cash award.  It would have looked bad if no one on 
our team received any award while all the other teams received at least one.

For Y2K I received a $1500 cash reward. This was because I wrote a ROSCOE 
RPF that could generate COBOL paragraphs that could be performed to handle 
conversions for four different date formats.  All the user had to do was 
select the format for the date and enter the name of the date field.  They 
could do this for each date in one database and the RPF generated convesion 
code for all of their dates that they entered. I also wrote another RPF that 
everyone still uses to this day to generate compile JCL for maps, online 
COBOL programs, batch COBOL programs, assembler programs, all with or 
without access to Datacom DB databases.  This RPF sometimes breaks when the 
systems people change the compile procs.  I don't know if they have anyone 
left who could fix it if it breaks.  As usual management was not interested 
in having me train any replacement.

After 2001 though it was all downhill.

I hope you enjoy your retirement and receive some appreciation from other 
quarter of your life.
```

```
Humanity to ensure its survival. That's just acceptance of a big picture. 
The machine can't run without cogs and it is a poor Engineer indeed who 
doesn't value them. Why would you want to think the machine will stop if you 
are not there? That's a pretty awesome responsibility... A fundamental rule 
for getting yourself advanced is to train your replacement.  (This is good 
for you, the new replacement and the overall machine...)

As for a "total lack of loyalty", There are many "loyal" employees. Most of 
them are working in companies that treat them right. :-)

>  The effects are visible on the news
> every evening.
>

 I agree the Evening News is generally depressing but that's not really 
because of the attitude of employees in companies. The News agencies don't 
see "good news" as being of interest and they make a living out of 
dispensing alarm.

Although I write an occasional guest Editorial for our local paper (when 
invited to), I don't generally subscribe to it. (I know... my bad :-))  I 
get the main news off TV and reading the papers just tends to make me sad. 
It is interesting that not knowing about death, destruction and mayhem 
actually improves the quality of your life. It isn't that I CAN'T deal with 
it, it is just that I get tired of HAVING to... Try not reading the paper 
for a week and see how much better you feel... :-)

>>
>> Sure, I get a catalog of gifts to select at each milestone
…[26 more quoted lines elided]…
> cynic and a stoic.

It is neither cynical nor stoical to ascribe to this. (However you may be a 
cynic and  a stoic for other reasons... :-)) Again, it is just realistic. It 
is not expecting a positive or a negative outcome, although it prepares you 
better for a negative one.

Speaking only for myself, I don't have expectations and I'm far from being 
cynical or stoical.

The secret is in the preceding sentence in the quote; be happy with what you 
have and enjoy what is to come.

Pete.
```

```
Charles Hottel wrote:
> "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote in message
> news:9b8kqnF974U1@mid.individual.net...
…[48 more quoted lines elided]…
> expectations.

I understand how you feel and it is perfectly reasonable.

It does make me wonder though how important a title is to us.

("What's in a name? That which we call a rose. By any other name would smell 
as sweet...." - W Shakespeare, 'Romeo and Juliet')

I once worked with a senior manager who made it a practice to invent new 
grandiose sounding titles and award them to people. Employees were very 
proud of these titles and often had them on a little wooden mount on their 
desk or in a frame on the wall.

They became like currency with people working to get the title of their 
Boss, who was working to get the title of his Boss and so on. It meant 
people could be promoted without actually getting any more money, just a 
promotion in perceived status. However, status was SO important that 
competition for it was fierce. People actually got a monetary raise about 
every two years but they were still striving for promotion throughout that 
time.

I remember introducing an Analyst to someone once  and later being taken to 
task privately by the Analyst concerned because he was a "Senior" Analyst. I 
never made that mistake again. (Didn't introduce him to anyone else... :-) 
No, just joking...).

Perhaps it is because we allow our work to define us (whether we should or 
not) and so the title that defines the worker becomes very personal.

Whatever the reason, it is important to take care with people's job title.


Pete.
```

```
Alistair Maclean wrote:
> On Aug 20, 11:52 pm, "Pete Dashwood"
> <dashw...@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
…[18 more quoted lines elided]…
> Tester.

Alistair, it is not dishonest to change the title you had to more accurately 
reflect what you did.

If you were employed as a "Programmer/Analyst" but your job involved large 
amounts of unit and system testing  it is not dishonest or misleading to 
change the title to be: "Programmer/Analyst/Tester".  Or "Programmer/Analyst 
then Tester"  ("Initially employed as P/A but later duties involved 
co-ordination of volume system testing..." or whatever...

I understand the problem with actual jobs being nothing like the "title" you 
applied for. It has happened to me too. Usually it is because Agencies 
simply don't understand what we do and certainly don't understand the finer 
points of it.

If you are applying, for example, for a "TESTER" position, and you have 
experience doing that, Change your CV to stress THAT experience. Don't lie, 
just shift the focus.

(I have separate copies of my CV for Banking and Insurance, for example. The 
underlying information is exactly the same (and true) but the presentation 
and focus is different if applying for a Banking or Insurance position.)

You can't really expect a single CV presentation to be all things to all 
people. Fora CV to work it has to trigger interest in the reader. The better 
it relates to what they are interested in, the more chance you have of being 
interviewed.

Remember that these days they use software to search for keywords. Make sure 
you have the keywords in your appplication.

Pete.
```

<a id="t-b88150c178"></a>
## Thread: OT: Do we get the Managers we deserve? — 33 this month / 34 total

_Topics: [`Off-topic and spam`](topics.md#spam)_

### OT: Do we get the Managers we deserve?

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2011-08-19T01:10:56+12:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<9b4hb2FkvsU1@mid.individual.net>`

```
Charlie's recent post about the last indignity made me think some more about 
Management and Staff.

Good managers realise that staff are the most important resource in any 
organization, and value them accordingly, but most of us have experienced 
Managers who don't share that view and see staff as easily replaceable 
cannon fodder who can and should be treated shabbily, bullied, intimidated 
and back stabbed whenever possible.

While I'd like to think such "managers" don't have much of a career path, 
there are enough of them around to give cause for alarm.

So what can people do about it?

Does bad management persist because we allow it to?

Suppose people just refused to accept bullying, unfairness, and 
intimidation? (I have done this on a number of occasions but as a skilled 
contractor who can easily move down the road to another place, and actually 
expects to do this, even on the "good" sites, when the project is finished, 
perhaps it easier for me to take a stand than for permanent company 
employees who are signed up for the duration.)

How can Staff raise issues with their Management in a way that will ensure 
their cards are not marked for the future? How do you deal with a petty, 
vindictive, mean-spirited manager, without actually destroying any chance of 
you having a future in the organization?

Doesn't this present a political minefield that IT guys are generally not 
trained, by job or nature, to be good at?

The first step is to realize that your pocket Napoleon is answerable to 
somebody. He may think he's God but there are higher powers.

In fact, here's a few thoughts I remembered, that might come in handy if you 
are working for a bad manager:

1. Nothing is true unless it is written down. When he asks you to do 
something that is blatantly stupid, send an email to him and request 
confirmation that you have understood correctly what he wants. If you see 
problems, observe politely that you have reservations (list them) but you 
respect his right to manage and will do whatever he requires, as soon as he 
has confirmed it by reply. If he doesn't respond, don't do it. When he pulls 
you up because it isn't done, tell him you are awaiting confirmation from 
him. If his voice starts to rise and he tells you to "damn well do it", play 
the broken record: "Just as soon as you confirm in writing by responding to 
my mail." Point out, without becoming angry, that you have severe 
reservations about it and therefore need it to be clear that he is 
requesting it. This is a fair and reasonable position. If it comes to an 
impasse, see point 2.

2. You have an impasse. It could be about anything, but it has reached a 
point where neither party will budge. Tell him you require the issues to be 
raised to the next level of management. DO NOT go behind his back; make it 
clear that, as no further compromise seems possible, the only way to resolve 
the issue is by a directive from above. Understand that the predilection of 
his Boss will be to support him, not you (if his Boss is any good - good 
managers support their staff, but the really good managers support ALL their 
staff and are more concerned with what's right rather than who's right.) 
Nevertheless, if the guy is being an arse and trying to enforce something 
that is obviously unreasonable, his Boss will realise it and you can win. 
NEVER do something you feel is morally or legally wrong, just because you 
were told to. The Nuremburg defense didn't work in Nuremburg and it won't 
work in your office. However, state clearly that you cannot comply and why 
you can't. Genuinely seek to find a compromise, but if you can't, raise the 
issue to the next level.

3. It is natural for Human Beings to like and dislike each other. You are 
allowed to dislike your Boss, but he is the Boss. He has a right to manage 
(or even mismanage, as the case may be...). Don't be rude to him (or anybody 
else - heightened emotions are no excuse for bad manners), but accept that 
you won't always be able to stay calm, objective, and polite, even though 
you should strive to. Try and minimise lapses in this regard :-)
As a general rule, don't hit anybody... :-)

4. NEVER apologise unless you are sorry.

5. If you are working for a bad manager, don't look to him for approval or 
praise. He is not a role model you need to emulate or really have any truck 
with. Polite, honest, but distant. Don't denigrate him to others (in fact, 
it is really bad practise to denigrate any of your colleagues and it will 
come back to bite you if you do. Applaud what they do right, no comment on 
what they do wrong, except in the sense of analysing to prevent a 
repetition.)

Looking back over a long career I can honestly say there is a case for the 
proposition that we generally get the managers we deserve. If you allow 
yourself to be bullied and intimidated in the workplace, or accept stupid, 
pointless assignments, how can you then blame your manager?

All comments looked forward to... :-)

Pete.
```

#### ↳ Re: Do we get the Managers we deserve?

- **From:** "Charles Hottel" <chottel@earthlink.net>
- **Date:** 2011-08-18T15:17:40-04:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<5oudncAKepK8-NDTnZ2dnUVZ_i2dnZ2d@earthlink.com>`
- **References:** `<9b4hb2FkvsU1@mid.individual.net>`

```

"Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote in message 
news:9b4hb2FkvsU1@mid.individual.net...
> Charlie's recent post about the last indignity made me think some more 
> about Management and Staff.
…[95 more quoted lines elided]…
>

I pretty much had to communicate with my supervisor via my project leader. 
I only met my next to last supervisor two  times and that was for mid-year 
or year-end reviews that he was required to complet before a certain date. 
He had to do this or else his supervisor would get on his case.

So here is the scenario of my last big project called CARES (COBOL Analysis 
and Report Extraction System) which I have mentioned here before.  This 
experience led me to make the decision to retire even though I wanted to 
work at least two more years.  Apparantly several people before me had not 
been able to fix the problems with this project.  You may recall that I had 
to learn Access 2003 and VBA etc. before stsrting actual work on this 
project.  This learning was was completed in early February 2011.

There were seven Access databases plus one Access database for each COBOL 
program.  The latter contained only  a table which held the source code for 
the program that was to be processed.  From February until the middle of May 
I sucessfully analyzed and documented one of the databases and 25 % of 
another database.  The documentation contained information on every database 
object contained within the database and was extensive.  On the first 
database I found that some of the VBA code was more advanced that what I had 
learned during the training phase.  This code invoked Window's functions in 
Window's .dll libraries. By doing research on the internet I was able to 
determine that this code came from a book titled "Access Cookbook" from 
O'Reilly.  I bought the book, read a couple of chapters, figured out the 
code and documented it.  This was the 5th book that I purchased at my own 
expense without reimbursement and of course this all took some time.  While 
waiting for the book I documented other parts of the database including 
figuring out that the developer had used an Access option to hide some of 
the tables.

So now it is the middle of May.  My supervisor emails my project leader 
asking to see a working demo of the system sometime in the next two weeks. 
My project leader forwards the email to me with his comments and questions. 
Remember all communication between me and my supervisor is done via my 
project leader and I do not see what my project leader says to my 
supervisor.  Of course my project leader should already be up to speed 
because I send him detailed status reports every week, but I have good 
reason to suspect that he does not read them or only reads parts of them.

My response was that it took three people three years to develop this system 
and that it was unreasonable to expect a working demo this soon.  I said it 
might take me a year to a year and a half to analyze it all and get it all 
working because from what I had seen so far it was in pretty bad shape.  I 
said that it was possible that with more time I would become more proficient 
at reading the VBA code, so I might finish a little earlier than a year. It 
was hard to give an accurate estimate.  I included with this an Excel 
spreadsheet that I had sent to them back in February that showed that this 
was not a small system.  In fact there were 165 tables, 76 queries  (many 
contained several sub queries and had to be printed in a 7-point font in 
order to fit on a sigle page), 52 forms, 15 reports, 52 form modules (in 
VBA) ,  54 VBA modules, 1 class module and 8 macros.  All together there 
were 17,011 lines of VBA code. Beside this there was one database for each 
program that held its source code.  I mentioned that I had not yet reached 
the part of the system that parsed the COBOL source code and that that code 
was very likely to be complcated.

The response from the supervisor was "I still need a working demo in two 
weeks".  I spent a sleepless night thinking about what I could do and how to 
respond.  In the morning I had a tightness in my chest and a throbbing in my 
head.  I became afraid that I might have a heart attack or a stroke.  That 
was when I sent an email saying that I had decided to retire, that the 
retirement people would need three months notice to process my paperwork, 
and so my retirment date would be August 1, 2011.  I waited anxiously for a 
day and a half without hearing any response.  Finally I called my project 
leader on the telephone and it became obvious that he had not yet read my 
email, so I informed him verbally of my intention to retire.  He said, ok, 
congratulations, I won't be that far behind you because I turn 55 in 
December 2012.  He said he would let my supervisor know and that he would 
give the project to someone else and I should stop working on it.

Fast forward to the middle of July.  I received another request for a 
working demo. I mentioned that I had been told to stop working on the 
project.  I said the system still did not work and the only demo I could 
provide would confirm that fact.  That was the last communication I had 
concerning the project.

I was disappointed with management on so many levels that I find it hard to 
summarize or itemize all of my complaints.  Back in the 1990's my management 
was savy and smart, but since Y2K the managment has gone downhill 
drastically.  I hope this story at least conveys some of the dysfunction 
that I had to deal with.

I did not want to go to my supervisor's supervisor because she was new, I 
did not know her, and beside she supervised somewhere between 500 to 700 
employees and contractors combined.  I might as well yell at a brick wall 
while pissing into the wind at the same time.
```

##### ↳ ↳ OT: Re: Do we get the Managers we deserve?

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2011-08-19T11:54:16+12:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<9b5n19Fjf2U1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<9b4hb2FkvsU1@mid.individual.net> <5oudncAKepK8-NDTnZ2dnUVZ_i2dnZ2d@earthlink.com>`

```
Charles Hottel wrote:
> "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote in message
> news:9b4hb2FkvsU1@mid.individual.net...
…[186 more quoted lines elided]…
> at a brick wall while pissing into the wind at the same time.

Thanks for the posts, Charlie. There are some perfect examples of poor 
management in what you wrote, but there are things you could do better too.

For example, NEVER allow a manager to stress you out to the point where it 
could affect your health. I've had bullying managers who said to me: "I want 
this on my my desk by Thursday" (Tough guy, street smart showing the plebs 
that he was not to be trifled with.) My response became different as I 
acquired more experience...

1. As a young, eager-to-please programmer: "I'll do my very best."  (work 
all night for several nights and have it there by Thursday).
2. After some years of doing the above and realizing it actually made no 
difference to anything: "I don't know if it can be done by Thursday, but 
I'll get as much of it done as I can."
3. After actually becoming a professional and realizing that the difference 
between amateurs and professionals is that professionals pace themselves, do 
not allow themselves to be over stressed and work steadily through problems 
making sure that proper sleep is obtained to keep the engine running and 
realizing that very few problems in commercial data processing involve 
people dying if they are "late": "Well, good luck with that. Hope you find 
someone who can do it by Thursday. I can't."

This last usually evinced: "What do you mean? I need it by Thursday."

"Why? Will the world end if you have it for Monday? I'm telling you that I 
cannot do it by Thursday and I can't. I can't do it because.... <list of 
real reasons>. Now, if someone else is prepared to accept it and undertake 
to do it by Thursday, then good luck to them. Would you rather I said "Yes, 
Sir" knowing full well it won't be there, or would you rather have an honest 
appraisal that allows you to manage it?"

This is part of the process of "training" your manager. He needs to 
understand that demanding something won't make it happen, that you can be 
trusted to be honest with him, and that you will always do your best to 
meeet REALISTIC requirements.

The other side of that coin is when YOU become a manager. As you are not 
stupid, and have been a programmer for a good number of years, you know much 
better than to demand something by Thursday. "This is pretty urgent because 
<whoever> needs it completed so that <whoever> can do the <whatever>. When 
do you think you could reasonably have it ready?"

Unfortunately, there are poor employees as well as poor managers and some of 
them will try and sandbag you.

"Awwww... that's gonna take about six days." (You know full well it is three 
days work...)

"Are you sure?"

"Yep."

"OK, don't worry about it. I'll do it myself." .. or... "OK, I'll give it to 
<eager young programmer>.  Now, perhaps you can start on this <piece of 
tedious, boring, shit work, that is not time critical>"

This is part of "training" your staff... :-) They have to learn that you are 
not a COI and you won't be bullshitted by them. Note that there are no 
emotional responses involved in any of the above. It is just taking care of 
Business.

As a manager, I have never demanded anything from anybody by a certain time. 
I have explained why it needs to be done and allowed them to negotiate a 
time period that will allow them to do it successfully. (You should want 
your team to succeed and support them in their efforts to do so. Make sure 
tools, environments, access permissions and everything needed to do the job 
are available, then make an agreement with them about the work and help them 
to keep that agreement. If it looks like it is not going to be there, they 
should tell you as soon as possible so you can address the implications of 
that, manage the impact, and get them help. Don't even begin to tolerate a 
blame culture; don't blame people for failure and don't allow them to blame 
others. If things go wrong, seek to understand what happened and make sure 
it doesn't recur.)

I think you may have been a bit intimidated by the CARES project, Charlie. 
You are actually much more capable than you may think you are :-). Of 
course, if you are poorly managed, that doesn't help your morale or your 
capability. Attitude is a very important part of anything and part of the 
management role is to foster that attitude (by making sure that people 
succeed and thereby growing their confidence). Just imagine for a moment if 
you had had a manager something like what I have outlined. Do you think 
you'd still be retired?

This is the waste in industry. Good guys like you get shoved aside by 
managers who have never been trained as managers and don't know the first 
thing about managing people. They percolate up the hierarchy because they 
have to get salary increments and the only way that can happen is by 
promoting them. The Peter Principle states that people get promoted to their 
level of incompetence, but it is actually much worse than that. In some 
places people get promoted way BEYOND their level of incompetence and end up 
failing to do jobs that have a significant effect on the organization as a 
whole, and a devastating effect on the teams they are supposed to be 
managing.

I was doing some management mentoring with a manager here a few nights ago. 
He is a good person and a good manager (not in the IT industry) but was 
having some trouble with one or two team members.  He came to the 
realization that he simply wasn't trained to deal with people (in this case, 
women :-)) and had never seen it as part of his job requirement. He WANTED 
to be a good manager but he was at a loss. We chatted for a while and I 
suggested a few courses of action. He said it sounded a bit "touchy feely". 
(This is a Rugby player...) I thought about it and he was right. But the 
fact is that sometimes you need to be "touchy feely" in order to relate. I 
asked him if he was capable of doing it or would he just feel embarrassed. 
He agreed to try and we'll see what happens during the coming week :-). In 
fact what he refers to as being "touchy feely" is really just being honest 
and up front. But many people (especially poor managers) have problems with 
that. Instead of being real they promote an image of themselves that they 
learned from movies. They see proper communication as "weakness".

I think there is a place for a "Management Handbook" to help people who find 
themselves escalated to the point where they need to manage others and 
haven't been trained to do so. With the drop off in the economy and 
consequent lessening of work for me I am working on a novel that will take 
the rest of this year. But after that I'll review and update the previous 
handbook I shelved and see if I can get all this encapsulated wisdom into a 
hard copy before I kick off to big Computer Room in the sky... :-)

Enjoy your retirement, Charlie. It is the start of a new phase in your life.

Pete.
```
