```
Kerry Liles" <"kerry.liles[minus_the_spam] wrote:
> On 8/18/2011 8:51 PM, Charles Hottel wrote:
>> "Pete Dashwood"<dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>  wrote in message
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
>>>>>
<previous snipped>
>
> Many years ago, I was a manager in a very stressful situation (as were
> all of the department heads) and I often had coffee with the
> Accounting Manager - who was really under tremendous pressure.

But what, exactly, was the pressure? Pressure to perform, pressure of 
deadlines, pressure of looming redundancy? All of these are typical 
pressures for management and staff and ALL of them can be addressed and 
nullified. Problems in staff relations and problems in management are just 
another part of the problem domain. They can be analysed and options can be 
formulated  to address them, just like we do with systems. (Except that with 
people there are some more skills involved...)


> After
> hearing his complaints for the zillionth time, I asked him if he had
> ever seen that old ventriloquist act: Edgar Bergen and Charlie
> McCarthy... he said "sure". I asked him: "so, who is the dummy here?"

See, you were "outside" his problems so you were able to realize that he was 
just being the effect of the situation, like a piece of flotsam or a 
ventriloquist's dummy. As soon as he was able to get over his current 
perception and take responsibility for the situation he was in, he was able 
to resolve it.

A lot of (generally poor) management control stems from manipulating 
perception. You THINK you have no options (because that's what they WANT you 
to think... )The reality is that we DO have options, we just don't always 
have the confidence to exercise them.

Doubt feeds on our hesitation and undermines our capability. ("How will I 
pay the mortgage?" ..."What If I'm unemployed?"... and so on.)

Try knocking round the planet a bit. Try being in strange countries and 
cities with only the clothes you stand up in. After you've been shot at. 
bombed,  been robbed, gone hungry, and been  yelled at a bit, you start to 
realise that you are still here. Getting fired from a job or not having a 
job is really not in the same league...:-)

(Sorry, I'm not advocating everyone should go Hippy adventuring as a way of 
dealing with bad managers, it just worked for me, that's all... :-) There 
are many ways to arrive at self realization; that's just one of them. The 
important thing is to do the personal growth to the point where you have 
confidence in your ability to handle things. To this day, I think the 
personal growth of the people I manage is actually more important than their 
professional growth. And it is ensuring I do what I can to assist that, 
which allows my own personal growth to continue.  "Touchy feely"? Maybe. But 
it is important.)



> Within a couple of weeks he had quit and moved on and was happier. It
> took me several more months to heed my own advice and move on. Advice
> is easy to give; hard to take.

Only because you needed some time to relate his situation to your situation. 
It is usually easier to solve a problem when you are outside it. :-)

Advice isn't always easy to give... especially when you know that what you 
are advising will be painful.

That's why one-on-one advice (as in mentoring) is much better than general 
advice (in a book or seminar). It can be tailored to the individual 
concerned and takes into account their strengths and weaknesses, as well as 
the specific circumstances. It is no good advising someone to do something 
which they are just not capable of doing, even if it is the right course of 
action.

Bad managers exercise control by undermining your confidence, and promoting 
the perception that "That's the way we do things round here". Some of them 
will stop at nothing to keep you in line and they implement silly, petty 
rules that show they have control... "You may not leave the building during 
working hours" (Is it a prison camp?)... "You will take a break at 3:00 pm 
for a duration of 15 minutes." (Whether I need to or not? What if I'm in the 
middle of a train of thought and lose it and have to go back and redo it at 
3:15?"... and so on.

Good managers don't need to be such control freaks. They set goals and work 
with people to achieve them. It isn't about control, it is about motivation 
and satisfaction in achieving goals. "Success breeds success." It is an old 
adage, but a true one. In reality, success breeds confidence and confidence 
breeds further success...

Pete.
```

```
Charles Hottel wrote:
> "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote in message
> news:9b5n19Fjf2U1@mid.individual.net...
…[54 more quoted lines elided]…
> that I could accuratly estimate how low this project would take.

First off, Charlie, none of my comments are intended to be persoanlly 
critical of you. You were in a bad situation and you did your best.

Rather, I just wanted to explore some of the ways that people can deal with 
management and vice versa.

To your comment above:

1. It is perfectly valid to say: "I don't know.". As long as you follow it 
up by saying: "Give me a day or two to investigate some of this and I'll 
give you a much better assessment.".

(I think this is pretty much what happened, isn't it?)

2. The fundamental rule is "be honest". (Don't let other considerations 
affect your honesty. If you don't know, then you don't know. That does not 
make you incompetent or a moron or lazy or anything else. The very best 
people in the business don't know everything. Don't ever let a manager (or 
anyone else) use the fact that you didn't know and said so, against you. The 
alternative is to lie. When that happens serious damage occurs. Honest 
assessments can be managed; telling a manager what he wants to hear is just 
preparing the way to Hell, for everybody concerned.)


>  I
> did have to use the help system to look up many of the commands that
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
> working.

It is important to flag stuff that doesn't work and could never work. This 
eliminates the old: "Well, it was working fine before you got hold of 
it...".

Be prepared to demonstrate that it doesn't work, if anybody calls you on it.

>  I discussed this with my project leader on my last day and
> he finally admitted that he thought that the system had never really
> workes as advertized.


>
> I have been thinking about why I did not take the approach that Pete
> suggested above.  For one thing all of the power was on my
> managements side.

That's perception. You (plural, not "you" personally) are constantly 
manipulated to believe you have no power and the Boss has all the power. It 
is only when you work both sides of the fence that you realize that is 
completely untrue. Your Boss is answerable, just as you are. Your power lies 
in your ability to make him look good. If you refuse to work with him he has 
to explain why that is. It doesn't take too long for senior management to 
realize that a certain manager is not performing well. High staff turnover, 
missed  targets, general low motivation and morale are all signs that a 
manager needs some training...

Suppose a scenario where you had said: "I've looked at this and it is at 
least x months work to get it right. Some of it will probably never be 
right. You may need to get someone with specific skill sets to work with me 
on this, or it might be better to contract it out as a fixed price job. I 
don't like undertaking thngs where the work involved cannot be accurately 
estimated and where there is no clearly defined achievable goal, but of 
course, if you want me to work on it, I'll do my best." Put it in wring; 
remember, when dealing with dodgy managers, if it isn't written down it 
isn't true.

(Again, this is not personal, Charlie. Just looking at scenarios...)


>Also in the past I had trouble getting even
> minimual support from management, even when I could make an
> overwhelming case for it.

Nobody should EVER have to make a case to get management support. It should 
be a given. Their JOB is to support you! Obviously, there was some poor 
management going on here.


>  Perhaps I was afraid that they would say
> "This is because you are working from home, so now you must come in
> to the office every day".

Had they said that, you would have shown how it would make no difference. 
Perhaps a compromise where you come in once a week. If you are to get 
assistance on the project, it might mean that you have to be on-site, or 
agree to meet up with your co-worker at a compromise location, some of the 
time . The point is that the solution to the problem is not about personal 
preferences, it is about what is needed to solve the problem.



>That would have meant at minimum a one
> hour commute in the morning and a one and one-half hour commute in
> the evening.

Tough. Unfortunately, that's the job. Many people have to do worse than 
that. (I had a project where I had to commute on underground and overground 
trains that were crowded and disgusting for around 2 hours each way, every 
day. It made me think. When the job was done I never took another that would 
require that. It was the longest 9 months in my life. I wondered how people 
could live like that, but they do. And some of them do it for many years. 
Because they want to provide for their families and get their kids to a 
decent school and sometimes, because they don't realise that they have other 
options. Tele-commuting is nice, but if you need to actually go to the 
office then you need to go to the office (or work out something else).

>Perhaps subconsciously  I had already had more than
> enough and I was just waiting for a reason to move on.  I am not
> sure.  I do know that hard, agonizing part was in making the
> decision.  Once the decision was made I was surprise how comfortable
> I was with it and I never really considered changing my mind.

If you feel good about a decision after making it, that is usually a pretty 
fair indication that it was the right decision.
>
> I have decided not to ask for a replacement plaque, because I feel the
> government has wasted enough money on this already. I already see
> what my retirment has done to the stock market and a replacement
> plaque could easily send the country into a double dip recession.

:-)

Fair enough.
>
> I have decided to email my project leader and tell him what happened
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> are zero .

While the plaque (and getting it right) is important, the lessons learned in 
investigating the CARES system are much more important. I'd write a 
management report on the whole experience showing where things coiuld have 
been done better and suggesting different approaches for the future. Send it 
to your Project Manager and copy his Boss. Don't be personally critical, 
just show how the consequences of actions or inaction make life difficult 
and end up costing time and money. Try and do it with a genuine desire to 
get something positive out of the whole business. It will make you feel 
better and it might help someone else to avoid some of the shit you 
encountered.


Whether you do that or not, make sure you let it all go and move on.

Pete.

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Do we get the Managers we deserve?

- **From:** Alistair Maclean <alistair.j.l.maclean@gmail.com>

```
On Aug 20, 11:23 pm, "Pete Dashwood"
<dashw...@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:

> course, if you want me to work on it, I'll do my best." Put it in wring;
> remember, when dealing with dodgy managers, if it isn't written down it
> isn't true.

Wringing the neck of the manager would probably give the most
pleasure. I agree, having been screwed by a two-faced lying COI, that
getting some things (such as promises to pay overtime)
in writing is an essential. It won't do you much good but get it in
writing, print it off and take a copy home.

>
> (Again, this is not personal, Charlie. Just looking at scenarios...)
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
> management going on here.

As we are not fully aware of the COI's circumstances in failing to
provide the support required we can not, in all fairness, pass
comment. If one were an absolute bounder, a COI might have engineered
a situation where a disgruntled employee was engineered in to an
untenable position and allowed to resign rather than incurring the
huge redundancy bill in terminating his employment. I know of one case
where a casual pub comment encouraged management to review a CV,
finding a period of employment which the individual had not detailed.
Dismissal was for lying (by omission) in the job application. Saved
the company a bucket load of dosh and could have made the hapless
employee liable for compensatory damages to the company. BTW, it
wasn't me.

>
```

One other thing to think about.  When I was in the real Army the standard

there was no possible advantage that could outweigh the cost.  When I left
the Army and entered the contractor world the same concept seemed to be
the norm.  You didn't get "transfered", people just moved from company
to company and if you stayed in one place for more than 5 years your value
on the market started going down (it was believed that your skillset was
stagnating!)  I have been here for 23 years.  Which system do you think I
see as better?

With proper determination and application it is possible to break the Catch 
22 for getting a job. No, I won't tell you how, (it isn't free advice) but I 
have done it myself and I've seen others do it, and helped yet others to do 
it. (I have dropped hints here in other posts, over years, as this is a 
recurring theme...) As to my being "very naive"... :-) I am 5 years older 
than you and doing a job I love. Until three years ago I was working in 
Industry and there was no sign of people not wanting to employ me because of 
my age. (In fact, I made that work FOR me...experience and maturity are 
valuable assets when managing tech teams which are usually comprised of 
young people.) I believe I could be employed in industry (as opposed to 
running my own company) again tomorrow, if I wanted to. It suits me to be in 
New Zealand now, and that is a much smaller pond than Europe but there are 
still fish in it. Age has nothing to do with it; attitude and analysis of 
what is needed and wanted  are everything. If you provide something people 
want, they will come.

The age barrier is in your head.

I intend to keep working until I want to stop, although I could have retired 
years ago.

>

> touch with the

>

>
.> In what way?
>
I said that places where they treat the workforce shabbily generally do not

that their diplomas are not worth much. As that is a prime performance 
indicator for a University, it confirms my suggestion


>>
>> I suspect that the Dean would have a different viewpoint. Maybe you
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> agree.

YOUR world, maybe. Try competing for a job against someone with a degree 
from Harvard or Yale... Unless you can offer a specific special skill, you 
are probably on a hiding to nothing...
>
>>
…[22 more quoted lines elided]…
> system do you think I see as better?

A very valid point. There are two schools as you noted. One says if you 
haven't moved, you can't get a better job, the other says if you move 
frequently, you are skittish and unstable (it is acceptable for contractors, 
but not permanent employees.)

People trying to make a career need to balance both. Decide what skill set 
you want to acquire and move to jobs that help you acquire that. There are 
contractors who really just want a permanent job at contract rates. They see 
extending their contract as desirable and  will try very hard to make it 
happen. Sometimes this is fine, other times it isn't. If I see a 
Contractor's CV with more than 3 years contiguous on one site (and 
especially on one project), I look at it carefully. Generally it gets 
rejected. I don't want people who have lost their edge and settled for the 
cuddle blanket.

However, the rules are not black and white and there have been exceptions. 
People considering a contract career need to be mobile and they need to 
exercise that mobility frequently. People in permanent employment, who 
intend remaining in it, need a different approach.

>
>> Life is for living.
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> All the best.

Same to you... hope your retirement brings some joy for you. :-)

Pete.

Check with Social Security to confirm, but consider this: You can
start collecting your early social security at 62, but you have a
whole year to decide to 'pay it back', and decide on a later age to
'start taking it.' That means you have another year to keep looking
for a job.

```
On Tue, 30 Aug 2011 14:36:47 -0700 (PDT), Ken <klshafer@att.net>
wrote:

>> ï¿½In any event, this is my last year here one way or
>> the other. ï¿½If I don't find a job I actually want to do by the time I turn
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
>for a job.

In 1979 I worked for Social Security.   At that time, someone getting
early retirement got his paychecks based upon that early retirement,
and if he worked, the paychecks were reduced.   At age 65, they
recalculated.   If he got SS checks for only one year, his paychecks
were adjusted to the size that they would have been if he retired at
64.

I don't know if it still works that way.    I don't understand what
"full retirement" means now.   Maybe it has something to do with the
above.     Because someone can still retire early, or at "full
retirement", or later - with higher payments for retiring later, it
appears that it is just a spectrum.   So what happens when they raise
the "full" age from 65 to 67?    I'm guessing they are just reducing
payments for people of all ages, so the feds can borrow more from
social security.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ OT: Re: Do we get the Managers we deserve?

_(thread depth: 6)_

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2011-08-22T13:04:27+12:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<9bdo8sFfl4U1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<9b4hb2FkvsU1@mid.individual.net> <5oudncAKepK8-NDTnZ2dnUVZ_i2dnZ2d@earthlink.com> <9b5n19Fjf2U1@mid.individual.net> <MdudncX7WrTRe9LTnZ2dnUVZ_oCdnZ2d@earthlink.com> <9baqemFsn3U1@mid.individual.net> <cc3c691a-96d7-4952-9bb6-1330ef31c073@h9g2000vbr.googlegroups.com>`

```
Alistair Maclean wrote:
> On Aug 20, 11:23 pm, "Pete Dashwood"
> <dashw...@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
…[24 more quoted lines elided]…
> comment.

I disagree. Management support of their staff is a fundamental. Staff must 
always be supported (unless they are wrong or have misbehaved, at which time 
a private session is called for to see what can be done to remedy things.) 
If staff need something and it is not possible to provide it, then a good 
manager communicates with the team and makes it clear why it isn't possible 
and lobbies HIS management to change the situation. Meantime, he and the 
team look for workarounds. Teams have a right to expect support from their 
management and there is NO excuse if it doesn't happen.  Why would any team 
want to work for a manager who sees them as cannon fodder, or just throws 
them to the wolves when his own arse is on the line?  There is much more to 
this, involving motivation, achieving goals, success and a whole string of 
other things, but none of them are possible if a manager genuinely doesn't 
care about or support the staff that work for him. It is not possible to 
motivate people unless you support them. Their enthusiasm lasts for five 
minutes until something goes wrong and they find their manager is not behind 
them.

Perhaps you are confusing "support" with "providing materials and tools"? I 
would agree that that is not always possible. Even then a supportive manager 
will raise the situation up the line and make sure that what is needed is 
obtained, or at least that the impact of not having it ois understood at 
senior level.

> If one were an absolute bounder, a COI might have engineered
> a situation where a disgruntled employee was engineered in to an
> untenable position and allowed to resign rather than incurring the
> huge redundancy bill in terminating his employment.

There is no redundancy bill if someone is terminated for gross dereliction, 
or violating the terms of their employment (stealing, damaging equipment, 
etc.)

The problem here is that the employee is disgruntled. Address that problem. 
Find out why. Sometimes people have a perfectly valid case for feeling 
disaffected. Sometimes they don't. Either way, giving them a hearing and 
seeking to remedy what was unfair can make a big difference in attitude. 
Incompetent managers seek to get rid of people they can't manage, and 
sometimes employ devious means to do it. My advice to anyone in that 
situation would be, don't walk... run! Get out of there and get yourself 
into a new site where you can start with a clean slate and make a better 
future for yourself. A constant battle to get redundancy simply takes more 
toll on your health than the money you (might) get is worth.

(I know someone in exactly this situation at the moment. I advised her 
months ago to simply quit but she is beset with all the perceptional fears 
that bad management propagate into people as a means of control: "Jobs are 
hard to get", "My qualifications are specialised and I can't get work 
easily, locally.", "We can't get by unless I work" (She is married with 
children and both her and her husband are hard working.). She has some 
special skills she could use from home and start her own business but, like 
many people, she is too fearful because the risk is too high.She wants them 
to fire her so she can get redundancy. They want her to quit because they 
want rid of her. Her manager makes her life a Hell and she is on the point 
of a nervous breakdown. It is a constant battle of Counsellors and 
Conciliators, trumped up reprimands for misdemeanours, politics and venom. 
She dreads going to work, hates her life and you can imagine how this spills 
over to affect the family. Now her health is being affected and she is being 
sucked into a dwindling spiral that saps her energy and ability. This has 
been going on for around 8 months now. What a waste of life! Even if she 
gets the redundancy, she will still need to find another job. I worry for 
her but at the end of the day, her future is in her hands. There is huge 
stress on the marriage and that is not worth any amount of redundancy.

Money is important but it should never be the focus of your life. If you 
have a family, that must be the focus. They need you happy and healthy, not 
worn out, drawn and dispirited. Bite the bullet: quit. You'll survive, and 
you'll be happier.)


>I know of one case
> where a casual pub comment encouraged management to review a CV,
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> wasn't me.

How sad is that? I can't believe that a pub comment prompted this. They 
wanted this guy out long before that. The pub comment just gave them the 
wherewithal to do it at no cost to them.

BTW, it is not good practice to lie on CVs. And a CV doesn't HAVE to account 
for every minute of your life. It needs to show dates and places where you 
were doing stuff that is pertinent to the job you are applying for. Mine is 
around 30 pages... I give them a 6 page summary (which is still too much) 
and show only the work I have done that is pertinent to the job I am 
applying for. If they are interested by the summary I then bring the full CV 
to the interview. Usually they aren't interested in my CV after we have 
talked.

(There was one occasion where an Agency insisted on having a full CV so I 
gave it to them, insisting that it should NOT be sent to prospective 
employers... I expected the Agency to extract what was needed for an initial 
contact. (NEVER expect ANYTHING from an Agency; especially not compliance 
with your instructions...)

When I arrived at the interview there were six people sitting across a 
table, each with a copy of my CV. The leader smiled and said: "Peter, we've 
all read your CV. We feel you should be interviewing us."  I got the job 
(and it was one of the best Projects I have ever managed, in a superb 
company with outstanding management at all levels) but I was still annoyed 
with the Agency...:-))


I don't remember ever failing an interview.. Obviously, since taking over 
PRIMA I haven't been applying for contracts, but I keep the CV up to date, 
just in case... :-) My advice to anyone putting a CV together:

1. If there are gaps in it, put a single line with the start and end dates 
of the "time out". Show it as "Personal development" or if  you are OK with 
revealing a bit of yourself: "Sailing across the Atlantic, trekking the 
Himalayas,  holidaying on a Kibbutz, learning to fly..." or whatever you 
did. Keep it true, don't make stuff up. If you were actually desperately 
trying (unsuccessfully) to get another job, you may say:"personal training 
and development" in the sense that you were getting experience at job 
searching... Besides, you did read some tech books and keep your hand in, 
didn't you?

2. A prospective employer doesn't have a right to every detail of your life. 
They have a right to honesty in your CV regarding the job you are applying 
for.  Don't feel compelled to answer every question if it is personal. Just 
say: "That's a bit personal. Maybe over a beer after we get to know each 
other...:-)"  Don't react to rudeness (sometimes they are testing you) and 
NEVER denigrate any persons or Companies where you worked previously.

3. Enthusiasm is like rubies at a job interview, but dont try and fake it. 
Instead, ask them questions about the work, the problems they would see you 
dealing with and so on. It is much more important to be INTERESTED than 
INTERESTING  at a job interview. If some of your previous experience (which 
might not be fully covered in your CV) relates to what they are doing, talk 
about it and see if any of that can help their situation. Avoid divulging a 
detailed solution. ("There's a whole lot more on that than we have time for 
here, but in principle, It looks like you may have some of the same problems 
they had at ... blah blah...")

BOTTOM LINE: Be honest.

Pete.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: OT: Re: Do we get the Managers we deserve?

- **From:** Ken <klshafer@att.net>
- **Date:** 2011-08-30T14:16:08-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<479c59c7-be4c-49ef-a43c-b770b0882176@b9g2000prd.googlegroups.com>`
- **References:** `<9b4hb2FkvsU1@mid.individual.net> <5oudncAKepK8-NDTnZ2dnUVZ_i2dnZ2d@earthlink.com> <9b5n19Fjf2U1@mid.individual.net>`

```
On Aug 18, 7:54 pm, "Pete Dashwood"
<dashw...@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
> Charles Hottel wrote:
> > "Pete Dashwood" <dashw...@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote in message
> >news:9b4hb2FkvsU1@mid.individual.net...

> This is part of the process of "training" your manager.

Pete is more right than even HE knows :-).

As I stated earlier, it is YOUR responsibility to 'manage your
manager', which often times does amount to exactly 'managing his/her
expectations." Make the hard things look easy, and make the easy
things look hard, is one way I have heard it put. In other words,
exude confidence that you can handle the difficult tasks, but never
sell yourself short, because what might be easy or straightforward for
you might be very time-consuming and expensive for others. Find ways
to let management know that.

A nice side-benefit of this approach is that in those shops where
manager turnover is greater than developer turnover, you can say to
the rookies when the new manager comes on board, "Damn! I just
finished training <former-manager> and now I have to train <new-
manager>!" Trust me, the rookies will be amused, and they will begin
to look to you for leadership.

Ken
```

OK, OK, Ok -- I've read enough. Charles, this 'CoBOL Analysis System'
was over-engineered and DOOMED from the beginning. Capturing 'source
characteristics' in a database is, in my experience, a fundamentally
flawed approach. My former organization recently spent six-figures
multiple-times-over for such a package. It blew up when analyzing a
couple of our largest CoBOL programs. Never good get it to work. They
finally, and quietly, sh*t-canned it. What can work: real-time
scanning with Unix or Unix-like sed/grep/awk/sh. As Pete will attest
to, the power of regex, in Windoze-speak. :-)


> <<snip>>


>
> My response was that it took three people three years to develop this system
> and that it was unreasonable to expect a working demo this soon.

'Scuse me for bein' stoopid, but WHERE ARE these three people who
worked three years each? Didn't _they_ demo it before they left? :-)

>  I said it
> might take me a year to a year and a half to analyze it all and get it all
> working because from what I had seen so far it was in pretty bad shape.

Your only mistake was dignifying your estimate with the word
'estimate'. Something like, "If three people working three years
couldn't make it work, WHO KNOWS how long it will take, or if it can
be made to work AT ALL!?"  Be wary here, you might scare off
management, and lose your opportunity to learn Access and VBA.

>  
> I said that it was possible that with more time I would become more proficient
> at reading the VBA code, so I might finish a little earlier than a year. It
> was hard to give an accurate estimate.

Draw them a bell curve with percent probability over time. 'See, there
IS a possibility, well, about 1%, that I can get it done under a year.
But look at the right-hand tail - there is also a 5% possibility I
won't get it done in five years, or ever.'


> I included with this an Excel
> spreadsheet that I had sent to them back in February that showed that this
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
> was very likely to be complcated.

Very, very good. Whenever possible, present facts to them, not
opinions. Also, point out that 17K lines of code would fill a 400 or
500 page book, which you are expected to know inside and out. Ask them
how well they can recite Chapter X of 'War and Peace'.

>

No job, and let me repeat this for emphasis, NO JOB is worth
jeopardizing your health over.

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Code and data conversion when migrating COBOL WAS: Re: Do we get the Managers we deserve?

_(thread depth: 4)_

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2011-08-31T13:25:18+12:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<9c5gruFs5nU1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<9b4hb2FkvsU1@mid.individual.net> <5oudncAKepK8-NDTnZ2dnUVZ_i2dnZ2d@earthlink.com> <a6bc95cc-0f63-411d-ac1c-6043430ae782@p25g2000pri.googlegroups.com> <mopq57dr5i8qudcoq16cujnmdkep9amar4@4ax.com>`

```
Howard Brazee wrote:
> On Tue, 30 Aug 2011 14:06:56 -0700 (PDT), Ken <klshafer@att.net>
> wrote:
…[14 more quoted lines elided]…
> buying) the new system from scratch.

Not ALWAYS, Howard. :-)

I have a number of very satisfied customers who can attest to this. With the 
right understanding and the right tools it IS possible to convert systems 
and programs FULLY AUTOMATICALLY.  (And VERY cheaply...) I spent three years 
designing, writing and refining software to do it and  it works very well. 
DATA CONVERSION (for example from Indexed files to a fully normalized 
Relational Database) is a matter of minutes. I had a client recently who 
converted over 800 ISAM files to SQL Server in a few days. This was 
estimated to be man-years if done manually. You can see a 15 minute video of 
DATA CONVERSION being carried out with real ISAM files becoming a fully 
loaded normalized RDB if you take the 5 cent tour at:

http://primacomputing.co.nz/COBOL21/demosandtutorials.aspx


CODE CONVERSION is a bit more problematic. All the programs which used to 
reference the ISAM data now need to reference the RDB. The initial impulse 
is to start converting the COBOL I/O verbs into SQL... THAT really doesn't 
work (and it is a tedious and error prone process.)
Instead the I/O should be stripped out to a separate Data Access Layer and 
the objects in that layer should manage the data for the applications, 
providing them with exactly the same data buffers they used to see when they 
used the COBOL verbs.

The PRIMA Toolset does that. It creates the DAL objects and modifies the 
legacy codebase to invoke the DAL object methods instead of using COBOL I/O. 
It does it fully automatically and it does it correctly and quickly.  Even 
procedural (non-OO COBOL) programs in the legacy base are modified to be 
able to invoke object methods from DAL. So, the legacy continues to process 
exactly as it always has, but now it is using the RDB. There is no SQL in 
the applications; it is in the Data Access Layer which is where it should 
have been all along...

Both DATA CONVERSION and CODE CONVERSION are fully automatic with the 
Toolset.

New development can use the DAL objects and can be in any language you want 
(including COBOL, if you want to continue using it). The DAL objects can run 
on a desktop or a web page so building SaaS services is really easy.

New development or package implementation can share the same database as the 
legacy so there is no need for overnight batch synchronization of the old 
and new systems. The Toolset can also analyse 3rd party databases and 
produce DAL objects for them so that the data separation layer is maintained 
and customized access to the 3rd party database can be easily written.

Keeping legacy running is often a very attractive proposition provided it 
doesn't cost you an arm and a leg and provided you can integrate it 
seamlessly with new development, then phase out or replace parts of it 
without being stressed for time. Everything runs; old and new.

And it costs much  less than you might think. It comes with a site licence 
and you can have as many people as you like using it, so that large 
workloads can be parallel processed.

Not to mention that PRIMA guarantees you a successful conversion :-)

Pete
```

On Aug 18, 9:10 am, "Pete Dashwood"
<dashw...@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
> Charlie's recent post about the last indignity made me think some more about
> Management and Staff.

Been away for a few days, so just found this thread... important
enough to reply to even if a bit 'stale'...

<<stuff deleted>>

>
> How can Staff raise issues with their Management in a way that will ensure
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> trained, by job or nature, to be good at?

Well, sure, but like with many things, even most things, "you get
better with practice." And the only way to practice is to try.


>
> The first step is to realize that your pocket Napoleon is answerable to
> somebody. He may think he's God but there are higher powers.
>

The first step is to recognize that you are an IT professional. And
though the project, and its budget, may be the organization's project
and budget, and your manager's project and budget, the profession is
OUR profession, and it is YOUR profession. Take ownership of that, and
establish that clear boundary around it.


> In fact, here's a few thoughts I remembered, that might come in handy if you
> are working for a bad manager:
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> confirmation that you have understood correctly what he wants.

You don't even have to use the word 'confirm'. Be dumb like a fox.
Feed it back in an e-mail, with a, "Gee, so that I know for sure, is
THIS what you want me to do?"

> If you see
> problems, observe politely that you have reservations (list them) but you
> respect his right to manage and will do whatever he requires, as soon as he
> has confirmed it by reply.

Remember that your lowly (relative) pay is insufficient for YOU to
take the risks. Management loves the word 'risk'. It gives them
something to manage (see Pete below on 'training your manager', which
I think of as 'managing your manager'.) So step up and give him/her
something to manage!  Say, "I don't know this, I haven't researched
that, poses possibility of <insert dire computational snafu results>,
and these are all risks. Which of these risks are acceptable to you?"

<<more along these lines deleted>>
>
> 2. You have an impasse. It could be about anything, but it has reached a
> point where neither party will budge. Tell him you require the issues to be
> raised to the next level of management.

Ahhh, this is when you tell him "Hmmmm... I'll be glad to do this, but
I do have grave concerns about the possible repercussions. I would
feel more comfortable if <insert his boss's name> were involved." Make
sure you use the word 'grave'. That shows your deep, deep concern for
the entire organization. :-) Again, like Pete says, don't go 'around',
but you can 'reach above', by cc'ing the next guy up the chain in your
reply e-mail. Sure, you'll irritate YOUR boss, so don't pull this
routinely, but used now and then it helps keep your manager in line.
(Remember: You are the one responsible for managing your manager.) And
if the answer is the same from Mr. Up-The-Chain, just make it clear
whether or not wearing a beeper is something you are willing to
do. :-)

<<related stuff deleted>>

>
> <<snip>>.

>Don't be rude to him (or anybody > else - heightened emotions are no excuse for bad manners).

And completely unnecessary. Usually the facts alone are sufficiently
incriminating. Do a little 'googling' and cite industry sources
wherever possible. "IEEE says right here in standard xyz that it is a
bad idea to place design-dependent criteria into a requirements
specification." blah blah


>
> 4. NEVER apologise unless you are sorry.

And you should never be sorry unless you were insubordinate. So never
be insubordinate. However, you may state that in previous shops you
were taught that it was appropriate, and even encouraged to 'challenge
management'. :-) It should always be OK to ask a question. Even if the
'concluding question' is : "Gee, after all the points I've made, are
you really, REALLY, *REALLY* sure you want me to do this?"

>
> 5. If you are working for a bad manager, don't look to him for approval or
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> repetition.)

Approval, praise, appreciation are nice to have. And really, I might
even trade X dollars an hour for them, where X is, uh, not an
exorbitant amount. But after all, you can't spend approval, praise, or
appreciation.

>
> Looking back over a long career I can honestly say there is a case for the
> proposition that we generally get the managers we deserve.


Ahhhh, my only disagreement with Pete, over a single word, what I say
is: "Looking back over a long career, I can honestly say there is a
case for the proposition tht we generally KEEP the managers we
deserve."


> If you allow
> yourself to be bullied and intimidated in the workplace, or accept stupid,
> pointless assignments, how can you then blame your manager?

And as Pete knows from some offline e-mail dialogue with me, when you
stick up for these principles then your manager may himself remove you
from your impossible situation. Be prepared to accept those
consequences if you refuse to be bullied. To restate the most
important thing I wrote here, it may be their project, but it is YOUR
profession. Treat it as such and defend it if necessary. And in the
long run, you will be doing your manager a favor. He/she just may have
a learning disability and may take a while to 'come around'. :-)

>
> All comments looked forward to... :-)

Good thread initiation, Pete...

>
> Pete.
>

On 8/19/2011 8:21 AM, jmoore wrote:
> I need to make fields with punctuation removed and combined. ie:
> FM-25 to FM25. What is the best way?

Well since "INSPECT .. REPLACING" doesn't do the removal part, you 
likely need to loop through each character and slide the remaining 
characters left one every time you encounter what you consider 
punctuation [this description assumes a left to right traversal]
```

Instead of replacing all the various punctuation characters by a
space, you could replace them with a specific individual punctuation
character that could then be stripped out.  However, as someone else
pointed out, one might as well loop through the lot in one pass as
INSPECT ... REPLACING would have to implicitly loop through anyway.

#### ↳ Re: Replacing and combine

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2011-08-20T14:07:24+12:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<9b8j6uFumdU1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<9e6a2ace-5941-4e50-b970-a6c205b727b5@t29g2000vby.googlegroups.com>`

```
jmoore wrote:
> I need to make fields with punctuation removed and combined. ie:
> FM-25 to FM25. What is the best way?

The BEST way is to use a regular expression which CAN remove your 
unspecified "punctuation". (in effect doing the "shift left" for you.)

You didn't state what "punctuation" you want removed. The example shows a 
hyphen. What about other symbols?

However, you would need to call a RegEx engine (because it isn't 
intrinsically available in COBOL). If you are running in a Windows 
environment you could use the Microsoft one. You have a choice of using the 
.Net engine or an older desktop one.

If you are using a version of OO COBOL (Fujitsu or Micro Focus) you can 
invoke it directly on an object instance, otherwise you need to call  a .DLL 
or execute a WSH script that uses the .Net  RegEx engine.

That's the BEST way (because it is more flexible than writing it in COBOL 
and the engine is designed for doing exactly what you are asking.)

However, if you don't really want the BEST way, just a way that is OK, and 
you don't want any of that new fangled 21st century stuff,  and using ONLY 
procedural COBOL, then here's some advice:

1. DON'T use INSPECT ...REPLACING. You will end up double handling the 
string to remove spaces.

2. Use TWO strings and a control loop...    The OUTSTRING must not be 
defined as shorter than the INSTRING. Here's some (almost) code...
      MOVE SPACES TO OUTSTRING
      SET K = 1
       PERFORM
            VARYING J
                  FROM 1
                       BY  1
                 UNTIL J > [length of INSTRING. Can be hard coded from the 
picture length for efficiency, but using a LENGTH function would be more 
flexible if the size of the input is ever likely to change...]
                       IF NOT (INSTRING (J:1) = "-" [OR anything else you 
want removed])
                            MOVE INSTRING (J:1) TO OUTSTRING (K:1)
                            SET K UP BY 1
                       END-IF
       END-PERFORM
       MOVE OUTSTRING TO INSTRING   [so references to the string are still 
good. OUTSTRING is really just a work area.]

Obviously J and K are defined as usage is index. If you use reference 
modification, as shown above, there is no need to redefine either string 
with OCCURS on it.

HTH,

Pete.
```

Pete Dashwood wrote:
>
> 2. Use TWO strings and a control loop...    The OUTSTRING must not be
…[18 more quoted lines elided]…
>

Alternative:

MOVE SPACE TO OUTSTRING
Move 1 TO J
PERFORM VARYING I FROM 1 BY 1 UNTIL I > LENGTH-STRING
    MOVE INSTRING(I:1) TO DUMMY-X
    IF (DUMMY-X ALPHABETIC OR DUMMY-X NUMERIC)
        AND DUMMY-X NOT = SPACE
        MOVE INSTRING (I:1) TO OUTSTRING (J:1)
        ADD 1 TO J
    END-IF
END-PERFORM.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Replacing and combine

```
HeyBub wrote:
> Pete Dashwood wrote:
>>
…[32 more quoted lines elided]…
> END-PERFORM.

Sure, that would work.

BUT, it is much less efficient because the CLASS Tests (ALPHABETIC and 
NUMERIC) call subroutines. The hyphen may also pass the NUMERIC test on some 
systems. Then there is theold "subscripting vs indexing" argument... In this 
case, because it is a single byte in a string, indexing is more efficient, 
but it really depends on how you defined your subscript...

Pete.
```

> HeyBub wrote:

…[45 more quoted lines elided]…
> "I used to write COBOL...now I can do anything."

You are now worried about efficiency ???

Whether a CLASS test calls a subroutine or not is an implementation
issue, but is likely to be more efficient than your:

         = "-" OR "?" OR ....... given how many could be needed

In fact a CLASS test may use a table lookup for each byte which would
be very efficient compared to a string of OR compares.

You also advise using reference notation against using OCCURS
regardless of any efficiency concerns.

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Replacing and combine

_(thread depth: 4)_

- **From:** Arnold Trembley <arnold.trembley@att.net>
- **Date:** 2011-08-23T00:23:11-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<nr-dnbSelPPVpM7TnZ2dnUVZ5sudnZ2d@giganews.com>`
- **In-Reply-To:** `<9bg5deFsirU1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<9e6a2ace-5941-4e50-b970-a6c205b727b5@t29g2000vby.googlegroups.com> <9b8j6uFumdU1@mid.individual.net> <FvmdndU0fPALLs_TnZ2dnUVZ_hydnZ2d@earthlink.com> <9bg5deFsirU1@mid.individual.net>`

```
On 8/22/2011 6:01 PM, Pete Dashwood wrote:
> HeyBub wrote:
>> Alternative:
…[21 more quoted lines elided]…
>

It shouldn't be that inefficient on System Z (IBM 370 and successors), 
because each class test should be generated as a single 6-byte TRT 
instruction (Translate and Test), although each TRT instruction does 
require a data table up to 256 bytes long.

If the field DUMMY-X is defined as PIC X, the NUMERIC class test should 
only allow the digits 0-9, and should disallow any sign characters such 
as "+" or "-".

It's also possible to define your own class in the CONFIGURATION 
SECTION, SPECIAL NAMES paragraph and create your own class test.

http://publib.boulder.ibm.com/infocenter/iadthelp/v7r0/index.jsp?topic=/com.ibm.etools.iseries.langref.doc/evfeb4ls17.htm

ENVIRONMENT DIVISION.
CONFIGURATION SECTION.
SPECIAL-NAMES.
     CLASS NAMECHAR IS
           'A' THRU 'I'
           'J' THRU 'R'
           'S' THRU 'Z'
           '0' THRU '9'.

IF DUMMY-X IS NAMECHAR
     MOVE INSTRING (I:1) TO OUTSTRING (J:1)
     ADD 1 TO J
END-IF


Kind regards,
```

```
Arnold Trembley wrote:
> On 8/22/2011 6:01 PM, Pete Dashwood wrote:
>> HeyBub wrote:
…[26 more quoted lines elided]…
> require a data table up to 256 bytes long.

You might want to check on that. For many years subroutines were used to 
implement CLASS tests, but as I haven't worked on System Z it may have 
changed. I agree TRT would be a logical choice but in this case it isn't 
needed any more than INSPECT ... REPLACING is needed. You are going to check 
each byte in the string, so to me a simple CLI  does that pretty well.

>
> If the field DUMMY-X is defined as PIC X, the NUMERIC class test
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> http://publib.boulder.ibm.com/infocenter/iadthelp/v7r0/index.jsp?topic=/com.ibm.etools.iseries.langref.doc/evfeb4ls17.htm

Yes, that would be another "interesting" approach, but my contention is that 
there is no need for a CLASS test at all.
>
> ENVIRONMENT DIVISION.
…[14 more quoted lines elided]…
> Kind regards,

There is no way that a TRT can execute more efficiently than a CLI on a 
single byte. (Check the execution timings...). The code I posted looks for 
the hyphen (and can be easily modified to look for other "single bytes" with 
COBOL OR. There MIGHT come a time where, if you had a largish number of 
candidates, the TRT might be better, but I don't think that, within the 
parameters given by the OP, that is likely.

Pete.
```

> micro-efficiency in deference to readability and simplified code.

I agree. Efficiency is not the prime criterion. But when you have two things 
that are otherwise equal in every respect, THEN you might as well consider 
it.

I'm not too worried about readability because I have moved on from seeing 
source code as really important. These days I'm much more interested in 
functionality and don't really care whether I have source or not. If a 
component behaves the way an Object Browser or its own documenation says it 
will, that's good enough for me. I don't really care how it is coded or in 
what language. Just so long as it provides the functionality I need.

Please note, that this whole debate is moot for me because I wouldn't use 
any of the solutions being suggested (including the one I suggested...:-)) ; 
I would use a Regular Expression. And I certainly wouldn't use COBOL...:-)

I don't think the solution I posted is any less "readable" than the one you 
posted, Jerry.

> for a gadzillion iterations of the two and found no detectable
> difference.

Yes, I spent some time investigating it on various platforms when it first 
became available. I agree the differences are slight in most cases, 
especially if subscripts are defined efficiently.

Pete.

## Thread: OT: Earthquake Wow! — 22 msgs

_Topics: [`Off-topic and spam`](topics.md#spam)_

### OT: Earthquake Wow!

- **From:** "Charles Hottel" <chottel@earthlink.net>
- **Date:** 2011-08-23T14:21:03-04:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<8POdnbtccJbQcs7TnZ2dnUVZ_jOdnZ2d@earthlink.com>`

```
Apparently a 6.0 around 90 Miles from DC.  My whole house  shook for about 
15 seconds and then the intensity increased for 5 to 10 seconds such that 
the chair I was sitting in started moving around.  Then it was over.  The 
water in my coffee cup, about a third poped out.  Floppy disks and things in 
piles fell off of shelves.  Even some books fell to the floor.  My computer 
desk actually came up off the floor. You could actually hear it and I 
thought maybe it was a nearby plane crash. Then I asked myself, could this 
have been a bomb set off in DC?

I can't even imagine a stronger one like New Zeland, Japan, and Hati had. 
There was just no time to react, even to go stand in a doorway.  It really 
brings it home in a personal way just how powerful these things are, 
especially when it is relatively shallow.  I have never felt anything like 
this before.

We once had 3.x earthquake in nearby Columbia, MD but I never felt it and if 
it was not on the news I would not have known about it.
```

#### ↳ OT: Re: Earthquake Wow!

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>

Charles Hottel wrote:
> Apparently a 6.0 around 90 Miles from DC.  My whole house  shook for
> about 15 seconds and then the intensity increased for 5 to 10 seconds
…[14 more quoted lines elided]…
> and if it was not on the news I would not have known about it.

Thanks for sharing your experience, Charlie.

I'm glad you suffered no serious damage.

Yes, earthquakes are frightening. Even when you've been through dozens of 
them it is still a bit frightening. You never quite know how hard it is 
going to be or how long it will last...

There was another one in Christchurch yesterday although the multiple daily 
shocks they have been experiencing are now subsiding a bit. There are still 
several hundred households camping in their backyards because the house is 
unsafe. And it has been really cold with freezing temperatures and below. 
For most people life is returning to normal (or as normal as it can be... 
"normal" means having water, electricity, phone, sewerage, and a roof over 
your head...). The chaos and devastation is like a war film but they are 
looking to the future and seem determined to rebuild. Many people have left 
(I don't blame them...) but there are some whose families have been there 
for several generations and they are not about to go.

Damage has been estimated to $40 billion  but the Government are 
surprisingly upbeat about it. Insurance policies have already been increased 
and there is likely to be a tax surcharge at some point, but mostly, it has 
been handled very well. We did get some incredible help from overseas (both 
in practical and financial terms... the Australians were outstanding, given 
they had their own problems at the time, and Japan was one of the first to 
arrive here with teams for searching the rubble. Ironically, those same 
teams were required at home a few weeks later.) I have never seen the 
country so united in support as it has been for Christchurch. I guess every 
cloud has a silver lining but it is cold comfort to the people who lost 
friends and family.

Can't help but wondering if there is some reason for what certainly seems 
like a huge increase in these events Worldwide.

Could the Christian Fundamentalists be right? :-) (We are entering the "last 
days" and Jesus will be here soon...)

Is there a covert weapons program going on somewhere that has run off the 
rails? (Not necessarily in the U.S.A... there are a number of States that 
could beundertaking such a program...)

Is the poor old planet just wearing out :-)?

Any ideas?

For now, I'm going with "unfortunate coincidence", but continual coincidence 
soon becomes "enemy action"... :-)

Pete.
```

On Aug 24, 5:13 am, "Pete Dashwood"
<dashw...@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
> Can't help but wondering if there is some reason for what certainly seems
> like a huge increase in these events Worldwide.

A fair question. One of the practices used to extract oil and gas is
the deliberate fracturing of the rocks around the drill mouth by using
explosives. That has been linked with a localised increase in
earthquakes and consequently has been banned in at least one location
(I can't remember where; tough). However, unlikely to be the cause of
your perceived worldwide increase. As the earthquakes occur along
fault lines and these are linked, slippage in one zone can increase
the likelihood of an earthquake in another linked zone.

>
> Could the Christian Fundamentalists be right? :-) (We are entering the "last
> days" and Jesus will be here soon...)

it easier to slip?

but he did a fine job in the part.

For anyone who hasn't read a Bond novel and thinks the movies are enough, I 
can tell you the books are infinitely better. Nevertheless, the movies are 
generally enjoyable and we keep going to see them...

I didn't connect the quote with Fleming but it may have been subconscious.

Pete.

The earthquake is now being called a 5.8.  A 6.0 would have been twice as 
strong. The cause is just plate techtonics with two plates slipping.  It is 
perfectly natural not supernatural or human caused.  Of course  we cannot 
discount the possibility that aliens are the cause :-) There have been 
several aftershocks but we have not felt them here in DC.

Apparently the standard homeowners insurance policy here does not cover 
earthquake damage.  You need a separate earthquake policy and on the news 
they say most people do not have it because it is very expensive.  The town 
of Culpepper, VA had a lot of damage and the main throughfare had to be 
closed due to unstable buildings.  Even the Washington Monument is closed 
due to some damage.  Some cars were damaged i.e. crushed by falling brick 
walls.  It is a kind of miracle that no one was killed.  In one store a man 
was juast about to go outside when bricks fell down outside.  They would 
have killed or severly injured him.  Timing is everthing.

We don't normally feel the Christchurch quakes. However, the Bay of Plenty 
is on the edge of a volcanic plateau, so we do get earthquakes of our own 
:-) Fortunately they are usually centred off-shore, (tsunami is a real 
danger for us and warning sirens are currently being installed) but every so 
often we get some on land. There hasn't been one you could easily feel for a 
few years now. Statistically, we have to accept that there is a likelihood 
of a Christchurch event occurring here (or in most places in NZ) ; we just 
hope it is not for a very long time.

Pete.

<a id="t-64e0c27f48"></a>
## Thread: OT: WSH and VBScript — 13 msgs

_Topics: [`Off-topic and spam`](topics.md#spam)_

### OT: WSH and VBScript

- **From:** "Charles Hottel" <chottel@earthlink.net>
- **Date:** 2011-08-23T18:20:16-04:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<7Iqdnaxte_nAusnTnZ2dnUVZ_qednZ2d@earthlink.com>`

```
I am learning WSH and VBScript.  My book has an example:

WshShl.Run "WordPad"
.
. .....staments that enter some text with sleep in between
.
WshShl.SendKeys "%{F4}"
WshShl.SendKeys "%{N}"

I get that next to last statment says to close WordPad and if I manually run 
Wordpad , enter some data, and press Alt+F4 a window will pop up:

__________________________
|    ! Save changes to document?  |
|    Yes   No   Cancel                   |
__________________________

So I assume that the "%{N}" is replying to that pop-up with a "No" reply. 
When I commented out this line the pop-window remains displayed and WordPad 
does not end.

So my question is how did the author know that shortcut combination would 
reply "No"?  I googled on "WshShel SendKey" and on "wordpad shortcut keys" 
but I do not see this combination documented anywhere.
```

#### ↳ Re: OT: WSH and VBScript

- **From:** Robert Wessel <robertwessel2@yahoo.com>
- **Date:** 2011-08-23T18:10:51-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<j0c857hlmlfn9oa2e5jd56aknhglov6kt0@4ax.com>`
- **References:** `<7Iqdnaxte_nAusnTnZ2dnUVZ_qednZ2d@earthlink.com>`

```
On Tue, 23 Aug 2011 18:20:16 -0400, "Charles Hottel"
<chottel@earthlink.net> wrote:

>I am learning WSH and VBScript.  My book has an example:
>
…[21 more quoted lines elided]…
>but I do not see this combination documented anywhere.


If I understand your question, it's the application that responds to
the Alt-N that "%{N}" sends.  It happens to be what the author of the
Dialog box in question decided to accept.  In older versions of
Windows it was usual to indicate the shortcut keys with an underline
(thus the "No" button would have the N underlined, but that was not a
hard relationship - a perverse app could put the underline on the N
and then accept Alt-Q instead), although more recent apps tend to omit
that fairly frequently.

MS has published style guides about all of this stuff:

http://msdn.microsoft.com/en-us/library/aa511258.aspx

http://msdn.microsoft.com/en-us/library/aa511453.aspx

http://msdn.microsoft.com/en-us/library/bb545460.aspx#dialog

And they specify that applications *should* assign a unique shortcut
(access key) to most buttons (some exceptions are OK and cancel, which
have standard assignments - enter and esc), and for "yes" and "no"
buttons they specify:  "For example, the access key for Yes and No
commit buttons should always be "Y" and "N", regardless of the other
controls in the dialog box."

Of course that doesn't prevent the author of an application from doing
something completely different.
```

#### ↳ Re: WSH and VBScript

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2011-08-24T16:58:25+12:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<9bjenkFkumU1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<7Iqdnaxte_nAusnTnZ2dnUVZ_qednZ2d@earthlink.com>`

```
Charles Hottel wrote:
> I am learning WSH and VBScript.  My book has an example:
>
…[21 more quoted lines elided]…
> shortcut keys" but I do not see this combination documented anywhere.

The answer to your question is that he knew it because it is standard 
Windows behaviour.

Look carefully. Both "Yes" and "No" have a little underline under the Y and 
the N.

These are "keyboard shortcuts".It means that if you press Y the system will 
activate the "Yes" button, and if you press N it will activate the "No" 
button. The script has opted not to save the document, as it is an example. 
Try changing the script line to: WshShl.SendKeys "%{Y}" and see what 
happens... :-)


When you construct buttons for Windows forms you can specify what the 
keyboard shortcut will be for that button. It doesn't have to be the first 
letter... For example, say I had a button labelled "Magic".  I can specify 
any of those letters to be the keyboard shortcut.  If I chose g, the letter 
g on the button would appear with an underline and if you pressed the g key, 
the system would behave as if you had clicked the "Magic" button.

Sometimes, when you have many buttons on a form, there can be a conflict 
between the keyboard shortcuts. So you choose a different letter.

Why doesn't Cancel have an underline?

Because "Esc" is the standard default for cancel. Any button labelled Cancel 
will be activated by default if you press Esc.

Note that, as a Windows developer, I can assign a keyboard shortcut to 
Cancel, just like any other button and I could use C, for example.

Good Windows form design will have a keyboard shortcut for EVERY button, but 
many developers neglect this or don't know how to do it. (It can be tricky 
as it varies between WPF or Silverlight where XAML is being used to 
construct the form, and standard Windows Forms.You can also specify at 
design time that you want a combination keyboard shortcut and Alt is usually 
chosen. So it is Alt+<whatever>. This is particularly important if you have 
input fields sharing a form with your buttons, as you don't want typed input 
to activate a button on the form..

In the Wordpad dialog box there is no possibility of confusion with anything 
else as there are no entry fields that require typing so it doesn't require 
Alt+<default key>.

Hope this helps.

Pete.
```

Working long hours has never bothered me.

Posting here does, sometimes.

It is entirely my own fault. It is easy to forget it is just Usenet and 
start taking it seriously, especially when tired or stressed. The 
perspective gets lost  and things which are really not important become so.

You can think too much.

"Thus is the native hue of Resolution sicklied o'er with the pale cast of 
thought. And enterprises of great pitch and moment, with this regard their 
currents turn awry, and lose the name of action."

I guess we get so used to taking work seriously it is too easy to take 
ourselves too seriously.

Nevertheless, I do enjoy the exchanges here and it is also good to see 
people getting helped sometimes.

I also find it is helpful to step out of a real problem occasionally and 
post here instead. I usually get back to work feeling better.

Pete.

### OT: System Environment Variables

- **From:** "Charles Hottel" <chottel@earthlink.net>
- **Date:** 2011-08-25T16:43:45-04:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<h4adnUgudvckLsvTnZ2dnUVZ_oidnZ2d@earthlink.com>`

```
In my system environment variables, the OS variable has a value of 
Windows_NT.  I don't know why this should be since the only OS my computer 
has ever had is Windows XP professional.  I suppose this is not really a 
problem since it has not caused me any trouble in the last seven years.

If I did want to change it how would I do it?  I assume I would have to edit 
the registry.  I tried changing it with MyComputer and ControlPanel but the 
change does not survive  a reboot.  I also tried a script that Pete Dashwood 
posted here last July, setEV.wsf, but it seems to only permanently change 
user environment variables, not system environment variables.
```

> Windows_NT.  I don't know why this should be since the only OS my computer
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
> user environment variables, not system environment variables.

Windows XP is Window NT version 5, following on from Windows NT
version 4.

It is probably not a good idea to change it as installing software may
check this and refuse to install if it fails to identify your system
using this.
```

#### ↳ Re: System Environment Variables

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>

Charles Hottel wrote:
> In my system environment variables, the OS variable has a value of
> Windows_NT.  I don't know why this should be since the only OS my
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
> system environment variables.

Charlie,

it is just a name. But it is an important name and part of the history of 
windows. The NT stands for "New Technology" and all windows versions since 
win98 have been within that "umbrella". XP stands for "Experience" 
(hopefully Microsoft had learned something from their previous efforts :-)) 
although it is supposed to refer to the "User experience".

Don't mess with it.

Pete.
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: System Environment Variables

- **From:** Robert Wessel <robertwessel2@yahoo.com>
- **Date:** 2011-08-25T19:38:39-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<82qd57hdnn89ef2ea66glhvph922nbjo87@4ax.com>`
- **References:** `<h4adnUgudvckLsvTnZ2dnUVZ_oidnZ2d@earthlink.com> <9bo6pkFrofU1@mid.individual.net>`

```
On Fri, 26 Aug 2011 12:13:40 +1200, "Pete Dashwood"
<dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:

>Charles Hottel wrote:
>> In my system environment variables, the OS variable has a value of
…[19 more quoted lines elided]…
>Don't mess with it.


Win98 and WinME are most definitely *not* in the NT branch of the
Windows family.  They are descendents of Win95, which is a descendent
of Win3.x.  The WinNT codebase (starting with NT3.1) is completely
new, and has basically nothing to do with the Win9x codebase (some
exceptions exist, for the more "application" level OS components, or
for certain device driver models).

The two lines coexisted for quite some time, WinNT3.1 actually shipped
two years before Win95 (and progressed through 3.5 and 3.51 before
Win95 shipped).  XP is strictly in the NT line, but was the first
version intended for "home" use, and it shipped about a year after
WinME.  The Win9x line continued for a considerable while after that
(in terms of being available for purchase, and support), since the
system requirements were significantly lower than for the NT line of
OSs.
```

On Thu, 25 Aug 2011 16:43:45 -0400, "Charles Hottel"
<chottel@earthlink.net> wrote:

>In my system environment variables, the OS variable has a value of 
>Windows_NT.  I don't know why this should be since the only OS my computer 
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
>user environment variables, not system environment variables. 


You shouldn't - you're running something in the NT branch of Windows,
so OS is correct as is (and has been that way since the NT3.x days all
the way to Win7).  Note that OS wasn't set in Win3.x and Win95, and I
can't remember if it was in either Win98 or WinME.

You should also avoid changing COMPUTER_NAME, NUMBER_OF_PROCESSORS,
PROCESSOR_ARCHITECTURE, USERNAME, etc...

Windows just sets all of those to the correct values when it boots (or
the session starts).

"HeyBub" <heybub@NOSPAMgmail.com> wrote in message 
news:_JmdnVkGWspnu8fTnZ2dnUVZ_qSdnZ2d@earthlink.com...
> Charles Hottel wrote:

I already tried that and my computer did work, perhaps because when I 
rebooted it changed it back to Window_NT.  Anyway I will leave it alone.

The VBScript book I am reading seemed to imply that you could use the OS 
system variable to check which operating system the script is running under. 
However I looked ahead in the and there seems a better way to check this:

'*************************************************************************
'Script Name: GetOSInfo.vbs
'Author:      Jerry Ford
'Created:     09/08/08
'Description: This script demonstrates how to use WMI to retrieve
'             operating system information
'*************************************************************************

'Initialization Section

Option Explicit


'Main Processing Section

DisplayData("gateway-sdxyc16")  'Set the computer on which the script will 
execute

WScript.Quit()  'Terminate script execution


'Procedure Section

'This function retrieves and displays operating system information
Function DisplayData(trgtSystem)

  DIM WMIServices, WMICollection, WMIObject

  Set WMIServices = GetObject("winmgmts:\\" & trgtSystem )
  Set WMICollection = WMIServices.InstancesOf("Win32_OperatingSystem")

  For Each WMIObject In WMICollection
    Wscript.Echo "Operating System:  " & WMIObject.Caption
    Wscript.Echo "OS Version Number: " & WMIObject.Version
    Wscript.Echo "OS Manufacturer:   " & WMIObject.Manufacturer
    Wscript.Echo "Service Pack:      " & WMIObject.ServicePackMajorVersion
    Wscript.Echo "SystemDirectory:   " & WMIObject.SystemDirectory
  Next

End Function
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: System Environment Variables

_(thread depth: 5)_

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2011-08-30T12:40:04+12:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<9c2pr5Ff8jU1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<h4adnUgudvckLsvTnZ2dnUVZ_oidnZ2d@earthlink.com> <_JmdnVkGWspnu8fTnZ2dnUVZ_qSdnZ2d@earthlink.com> <r_qdnWLc29KH9sfTnZ2dnUVZ_hCdnZ2d@earthlink.com> <9c1mcjFbftU1@mid.individual.net> <JLWdnbMMCLmgWsbTnZ2dnUVZ_u-dnZ2d@earthlink.com>`

```
Charles Hottel wrote:
> "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote in message
> news:9c1mcjFbftU1@mid.individual.net...
…[95 more quoted lines elided]…
> is right.

The price is indeed right. :-)

People like to jump on Microsoft, but they do provide some amazing 
capability for free. I downloaded  the express version of SQL Server 2008 to 
a VM the other day as part of duplicating a customer's environment. It is 
every bit  as good (in fact, better...) than the full version of SQL Server 
2005 I usually use, and have resisted upgrading.

I recently spent a couple of weeks working with PostgreSQL for another 
customer and, although it was good in parts, overall it was much harder to 
make things happen than with SQL Server. The problem with Open software is 
that no-one seems to be responsible for it. I needed a particular ODBC 
driver for PG and there was one, but it was buggy. I got the impression it 
was being written by enthusiasts after school and they weren't concerned 
about when it would be right.

Commercial software doesn't have that problem, but you do have to pay for 
it. UNLESS you can get it for free, like Express Editions from Microsoft. I 
give thanks every day for products like C# which are not only free, but have 
an enthusiastic user community who support them for free as well. The latest 
incarnation of C# is moving towards asynchronous programming designed to run 
on multiple processors. This language just gets better and better... and it 
is still FREE!

I started off with a free Express Edition of Visual Studio, but within 3 
months it had so blown me away I upgraded to the full version. (there wasn't 
that much difference but there were some facilities I especially wanted for 
web development.)

> I wish I had learned it before now.  If not for your
> posting about it and WSH I might not have realized it existed. Thanks.

You're very welcome, Charlie.

Most mainframe people couldn't imagine a world without JCL or REXX, yet they 
resist looking at the PC equivalent, even when they are working with PCs 
every day.

Scripting is definitely a very powerful tool for any system administrator or 
programmer to have in their toolbox. I have been integrating WSH into 
solutions for the Windows platform for a number of years now and am very 
impressed at the way it opens up possibilities.

For example:

If you use Fujitsu COBOL (NetCOBOL or PowerCOBOL) you need to install it on 
each machine you are using for development. This means a license for each 
machine and all the unwieldy nonsense of transferring the license if you 
move it to another machine. How much better would it be if you could install 
it to ONE machine, then use that machine as a "COBOL server" and run the 
compiler remotely from any machine where development was taking place? (If 
the "COBOL Server" happens to be a Virtual Image and the hardware fails, you 
can start it on another machine in seconds.)

(Micro Focus use the concept of a server very well with their COBOL)

It wasn't until I got into WSH and WMI that I started to realise it would 
probably be possibe to use Remote Procedure Calls (RPC) to activate COBOL on 
a remote machine. I spent an interesting afternoon experimenting and now the 
PRIMA Toolset can run the COBOL compiler locally or remotely, to instantly 
compile the code it generates.

I no longer have COBOL installed on my own development machine. (It is a C# 
environment with Visual Studio 2008 (I'm playing with VS 2010 at the moment 
to see if I need to upgrade. At this stage it looks like I would only need 
it for mobile and phone development, which I really don't have time to get 
into seriously right now.))

Instead, I run different versions of COBOL on different Virtual Machines, 
whenever I need them, to test things for various clients with different 
versions of COBOL. The VMs appear on my wireless LAN just like any other 
networked machine and I can run the COBOL on any one of them from any other 
networked machine, using WSH and scripts I wrote to support that. Without 
the power of WMI and WSH I don't think this would be easy. I can keep the 
VMs on a separate hard drive (in fact, I have a couple of them backed up to 
memory sticks...) and it leaves more space for development on my own 
notebook. It takes less than 20 seconds to bring a VM on line (for some 
reason which I haven't yet understood, the VMs boot into Windows in a 
fraction of the time a real machine takes...it could be that they are 
sharing some parts of the OS with the host, I don't know...))

I would strongly recommend any COBOL developers reading this to think about 
using VMs as development machines. You can snapshot the whole machine image 
in seconds, experiment and try out things you might hesitate to do on a real 
machine, because the Virtual Image is "expendable" and easily replaced. I 
wish I had moved into this environment many years ago instead of the five or 
so I have been using it.

Latest versions of Windows come with Virtual Machine support. I use ORACLE's 
VirtualBox and find it outstanding, but it really doesn't matter which 
particular form of VM you decide to use; the concept is what is important.

I know you spent some time looking at Java, Charlie.

You should be aware that WSH can host JavaScript as well as VBScript. 
Although JavaScript is NOT Java there are some passing similarities and I 
have to admit that sometimes I prefer JScript to VBScript.

It's probably just me, but I think there is a brutality about VBScript. :-) 
It doesn't have the elegant finesse of JavaScript. Maybe I'm being too 
"touchy feely"... :-)

At the end of the day, tools are tools and whatever works is good.

Pete.
```

Well the VBScript I/O seems pretty well designed compared to Java.  I/O in 
Java has been redesigned several times and it is potentially pretty 
complicated.
For Java I/o see: http://mindprod.com/jgloss/io10.html   It is complicated 
enough that one guy wrote a Java source code generator to help people get 
the right code for the options they want.  See: 
http://mindprod.com/jgloss/fileio.html

It has a lot of useful objects, like the Dictionary object, and the various 
Windows related objects, and they are easy to use.. It's Function statement 
is more flexible than COBOL's, and accessing file dynamically is much better 
than the way you have to do with mainframe COBOL.  Of course it has it's 
limitations also but I like that it seems much less heavy handed than Java.

What is 'easy' and what is 'hard' depends entirely on what you are

> The problem with Open software is

It may well be that you are unaware of who is 'responsible'. In many
cases it is a complete community, in others it is business based,
usually there is corporate support. For PostgreSQL there is financial
support from IBM. There is also EnterpriseDB which has a commercial
version of the free (actually free) PostgreSQL and provide additional
services and support (for money).

The PostgreSQL ODBC driver is a part of the project. It is easy to
find who is responsible, 20 seconds with google tells me the project
leader is David Page and lists the eight developers who work on it.

There are actually several version of the ODBC driver available going
back to 2001 so that you can match the version to that of the
PostgreSQL you are running. Did you post your 'bug' to the appropriate
forum ?


> Commercial software doesn't have that problem, but you do have to pay for
> it.

That is why it is called 'commercial'. You can get PostgreSQL as a
commercially supported version from EnterpriseDB, or get it free and
just pay for support.


> UNLESS you can get it for free, like Express Editions from Microsoft.

But is isn't really free, is it. You need to buy a Windows Server
edition and you need to buy CALs. And it certainly isn't 'free'.

> I
> give thanks every day for products like C# which are not only free, but have
…[35 more quoted lines elided]…
> (Micro Focus use the concept of a server very well with their COBOL)

You should note that you still need to buy a licence for each
developer that will use the software.

With, eg, PowerCOBOL it was necessary to have it available on the
machine being used because it was a GUI that ran locally. It may have
been possible to run it using, say, RDP or VLC, but only one user at a
time.

The same applied to MicroFocus COBOL if you were using their toolset,
such as the editor (spit) or Animator.

With both Fujitsu and Microfocus it would be possible to use the
compiler on a server under the control of, say, make to compile the
programs while developers used their own tools (editors etc, or even
Eclipse) to write the code which would be stored on the server.

In both supplier's case this would still require the purchase of a
licence for each user.


> It wasn't until I got into WSH and WMI that I started to realise it would

…[28 more quoted lines elided]…
> so I have been using it.

first.

…[16 more quoted lines elided]…

Pete Dashwood wrote:

> Instead, I run different versions of COBOL on different Virtual
> Machines, whenever I need them, to test things for various clients
…[10 more quoted lines elided]…
> the OS with the host, I don't know...))

I'm since many years using XEN and VirtualBox for VM's running
Linux/Unix based OS systems. As far as I've understood hypervisors the
OS in the VM is communicating with the hypervisor (not the acual
hardware/firmware) which might save it a lot of time in starting.

XEN is known for its special construct of its hypervisor which resembles
the hypervisor structure on mainframes e.g. sharing parts of its code
with the OS in the VM. Many other hypervisor cannot do that because
often the license of the OS (e.g. MS-Windows) forbids that. XEN in an
early development phase had access to the MS-Windows source code with
an academic license but that could of course not be used in its own
source because that interferes with its use of the GPL. 

> I would strongly recommend any COBOL developers reading this to think
> about using VMs as development machines. You can snapshot the whole
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> it.

Agreed, but the recommendation should not be limited to the use of
COBOL.
```
